Motion in Substance and the Flowing Accident Solution
Argues that motion in constitutive forms is impossible, proposes flowing accident for quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion cannot occur in constitutive forms and how a single flowing accident explains motion in quality.

Core Idea
Motion in substance is impossible; only accidents can flow.

This passage tackles a key problem in kalam physics: how can something change while remaining itself? The solution distinguishes motion in quality (which can involve a flowing accident) from motion in substantial form (which is impossible). Read carefully to follow the argument and its refutation of an objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: solution for quality, then proof against motion in form, then objection and refutation.
2
Identify the key concepts
Look for terms like 'flowing accident' (حال سيال), 'form' (صورة), 'matter' (مادة), and 'principle of motion' (مبدأ الحركة). Understand how they relate.
3
Understand the argument against motion in form
The author argues that matter only exists through a specific form; motion would require it to lose that form, which is impossible. Grasp why.
4
Examine the objection and refutation
The objection says matter could have successive forms. The refutation says each form gives a distinct essence, so there is no continuous motion.
5
Connect to broader kalam
This argument supports the view that only accidents can flow, while substances remain fixed. Think about its implications for change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا محيص عن ذلك إلا ما أن المتحرك في الكيف مثلاً له فيما بين مبدأ حركته ومنتهاها كيفية واحدة سيالة كما عرفت ومثل هذا الحال السيال الذي يتبدل أفراده على محله مع بقاء المحل بشخصه لا بد أن يكون عرضاً لتقوم محله بدونه فلا يتصور حركة في الصور المقومة لمحالها ( وأيضاً فمبدأ الحركة) أي ما تقوم به الحركة وهو المتحرك (موجود) لا محالة في زمان كونه متحركاً ( والمادة وحدها لا وجود لها فإنَّ المادة لا تتحصل ذاتاً معينة موجودة إلا بالصورة المعينة فلا يمكن ) حركتها إلا إذا كانت من مبدأ حركتها إلى منتهاها متصورة بصورة معينة فيمتنع تتحرك في الصورة بالضرورة، وقد يقال : تحصل المادة بشخصها إنما يكون بصورة متعاقبة لا بصورة واحدة معينة، فلا يلزم امتناع حركتها، ويجاب بأنها مع إحدى تلك الصور ذات متحصلة ومع صورة أخرى ذات متحصلة مغايرة للذات الأولى وليس لشيء من تلك الذوات المتحصلة حركة وانتقال من حالة إلى حالة أخرى، فلا حركة أصلاً
There is no escape except that the moving thing in quality, for example, has between the beginning of its motion and its end a single flowing quality (kayfiyya waḥida sayyāla), as you know. And such a flowing state (ḥāl sayyāl) whose individuals change upon its subject while the subject remains in its person must be an accident ('araḍ) because its subject is constituted without it. So motion in the forms that constitute their subjects cannot be conceived. And also the principle of motion (mabda' al-ḥaraka), i.e., that by which motion subsists, which is the moving thing, necessarily exists at the time of its being in motion. Matter (māddah) alone has no existence, for matter does not become a determinate existing essence except by a specific form. So its motion is impossible unless from the beginning of its motion to its end it is formed by a specific form; thus it is necessarily impossible for it to move in form. It may be said: Matter becomes determined in its person by successive forms, not by a single specific form, so the impossibility of its motion does not follow. The answer is that with one of those forms it is a determinate essence, and with another form it is a determinate essence different from the first essence, and none of those determinate essences have motion and transition from one state to another, so there is no motion at all.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation concerning motion in substances. It begins by presenting the only escape for motion in quality: a single flowing accident (kayfiyya sayyāla) that persists while its individuals change. Such a state must be an accident because the subject is constituted without it. Therefore, motion cannot occur in forms that constitute the subject (e.g., substantial form), as that would require the subject to be devoid of form. The passage then argues that the moving thing must exist during motion, and matter (māddah) alone has no existence without a specific form—it only becomes determinate through form. Hence, matter cannot move in form unless it retains a single form throughout, which is impossible for motion. An objection is raised: matter is determined by successive forms, not one specific form, so motion might be possible. The refutation is that each form gives a distinct determinate essence, and none of those essences undergo motion or transition; thus no motion occurs. The argument shows that motion in substance is incoherent.

Key Terms
حركة
كيف
صورة
مادة
عرض
حال سيال
مبدأ الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion in substance can occur through successive forms replacing each other rapidly.
Correction: The passage shows that each form gives a distinct essence; there is no continuous subject undergoing motion, only a series of static states.

Checkpoint
What is the proposed solution for motion in quality according to the text?
A) A series of discrete forms
B) A single flowing accident
C) The persistence of matter
D) The change of subject

Key Takeaway
Motion requires a continuous state that persists across change, which is impossible for substantial forms but possible for accidents like quality.

What does that reveal about the nature of your struggle against sin?

The Nature of Hyle: Potentiality and Actuality
Explains that hyle is potential and becomes actual through forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that hyle (prime matter) is purely potential and only becomes determinate through a specific form, and that its attributes depend on form.

Core Idea
Hyle has no actual existence without form; it is pure potential.

This passage lays the foundation for understanding hyle, a key concept in Islamic philosophy and kalam. The author explains that hyle is not an actual entity in itself but becomes determinate only through form. This principle is crucial for later discussions on motion and change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on hyle
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the claim that hyle is 'nothing but something potential' and that its properties depend on form.
The passage begins with 'وهذا الجواب' and ends with 'مجال'.
2
Identify the key principle
The passage states that hyle's unity, multiplicity, continuity, and discontinuity are dependent on form. This means these attributes are not intrinsic to hyle.
3
Consider the counterfactual
The author notes that if hyle were actually determinate in itself, it would not behave as described. This highlights the importance of the potential nature of hyle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الجواب كما ترى مبني على أنَّ الهيولى ليست إلا شيئاً بالقوة لا تتحصل موجودة إلا بصورة معينة لما ثبت عندهم من أن وحدتها وتعددها واتصالها وانفصالها تابعة للصورة، فلو كانت في ذاتها متحصلة بالفعل لما كانت كذلك وللبحث فيه مجال ( وأما المضاف فطبيعة غير مستقلة ) بنفسها في المفهومة ( بل) هي ( تابعة لغيرها
قوله : ( إنما يكون بصور متعاقبة كالبيت فإنه متحصل بدعائم متعاقبة لا بدعائم معينة. قوله : ( وهذا الجواب إلخ لو قرر الجواب أنَّ الهيولى ليس بمحصلة إلا بالصور المعينة، أما لو قرر بأنَّ الهيولى مع إحدى الصور نوع محصلة وبالصورة الأخرى نوع آخر وليس في شيء من تلك الأنواع حركة حال فإن الحركة متوسطة بين مخصوصة القوة مخصوصة الفعل وهاهنا إما مخصوصة القوة أو مخصوصة الفعل فلما بني على تلك المقدمة قال : الهيولى مع كونها متحصلة في نفسها بتوارد الصور لا بد لها من حالة متوسطة بينها إذا كان الانتقال تدريجياً . قوله : ( لما كانت كذلك أي لما كانت وحدتها وتعددها واتصالها وانفصالها تابعة للصورة لا يستلزم أن يكون متحصلة في نفسها فإنهم قالوا : إنها متحصلة في نفسها بتوارد الصور
And this answer, as you see, is based on the fact that hyle (al-hayūlā) is nothing but something potential, not becoming existing determinate except by a specific form, because it is established with them that its unity and multiplicity and continuity and discontinuity are dependent on the form. So if it were in itself actually determinate, it would not be like that. And there is room for investigation in this. As for the relative (al-muḍāf), its nature is not independent in concept, but rather it is dependent on something else. Statement: (It is only with successive forms like the house, for it is constituted by successive pillars, not by specific pillars.) Statement: (And this answer etc.) if the answer is formulated that hyle is not determinate except by specific forms, then if it is formulated that hyle with one form is a determinate type and with another form is another type, and none of those types have motion, because motion is intermediate between specific potentiality and specific actuality, and here it is either specific potentiality or specific actuality. Since it is built on that premise, he said: Hyle, despite being determinate in itself by the succession of forms, necessarily has an intermediate state between them when the transition is gradual. Statement: (since it was like that) i.e., since its unity and multiplicity and continuity and discontinuity are dependent on form, it does not entail that it is determinate in itself, for they said: It is determinate in itself by the succession of forms.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory note on the previous argument. It clarifies the underlying principle: hyle (prime matter) is purely potential and only becomes an actual determinate thing through a specific form. The unity and multiplicity of hyle are not intrinsic but follow the form. If hyle were actually determinate in itself, it would not depend on form in that way, but this is not the case. The passage then discusses the category of the relative (al-muḍāf), noting its conceptual dependence. It proceeds to examine two formulations of the earlier answer. In one, hyle is only determinate through specific forms. In another, hyle with one form constitutes one type, and with another form another type; none of these types undergo motion because motion is intermediate between potentiality and actuality, but here each state is either entirely potential or entirely actual. The gloss then notes that if transition is gradual, hyle must have an intermediate state, but this is built on the premise that hyle is determinate in itself through succession of forms—a premise that is questioned.

Misconception: That hyle has an independent actuality and its attributes are intrinsic
Correction: The passage stresses that hyle is potential and its attributes depend entirely on the form that actualizes it.

Key Takeaway
Hyle is pure potential; it only becomes actual through a specific form, and its attributes depend on the form.

The Relative and Two Formulations on Motion
Discusses the relative and two formulations of the answer on motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the relative (al-muḍāf) and two possible formulations of the answer about hyle and motion.

Core Idea
The relative depends on another, and motion requires an intermediate state between potentiality and actuality.

This passage continues the discussion by first clarifying the category of the relative, then examining two ways to formulate the answer about hyle and motion. The author analyzes the implications of each formulation for the possibility of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the relative
Read the beginning of the Arabic: 'وأما المضاف فطبيعة غير مستقلة'. Understand that 'al-muḍāf' (the relative) is conceptually dependent on something else.
This is a brief but important philosophical point.
2
Identify the two formulations
The passage presents two formulations of the answer. The first says hyle is only determinate through specific forms. The second says hyle with each form constitutes a distinct type, and none have motion.
Look for the phrases 'لو قرر الجواب أنَّ الهيولى ليس بمحصلة إلا بالصور المعينة' and 'أما لو قرر بأنَّ الهيولى مع إحدى الصور نوع محصلة'.
3
Understand the role of the intermediate state
The gloss notes that motion requires a state intermediate between particular potentiality and particular actuality. In the second formulation, each type is either fully potential or fully actual, so motion is impossible unless hyle has an intermediate state.
4
Reflect on the presupposition
The final part points out that the need for an intermediate state assumes hyle is determinate in itself through the succession of forms, which is a controversial premise.

Motion in the Category of Relation Follows Its Substrate
Sanusi establishes that motion in the category of relation follows motion in its substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion in the category of relation (al-idaafa) cannot occur independently but must follow motion in its substrate.

Core Idea
Relation has no independent motion; it rides on changes in its substrate.

In this passage, Sanusi explains a key principle: relation (al-idaafa) is not an independent category, so any motion in it must be dependent on motion in its substrate. He illustrates this with examples from quality, place, quantity, and position, then addresses an objection regarding the categories of place and position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage first without over-analyzing, then follow the translation. Get a sense of Sanusi's argument flow.
The passage begins with a conditional statement about the substrate's receptivity to more and less.
2
Identify the main principle
Find the sentence that states relation is dependent on its substrate in motion and change. Understand why: if relation changed independently, it would be an independent concept.
3
Examine the examples
Sanusi provides four examples: water heat (quality), place (al-ayn), quantity, and position (al-wad'). For each, identify the substrate that changes and how the relation follows.
Notice that in each case, the relation changes gradually as the substrate changes.
4
Understand the objection and reply
Sanusi raises the objection that al-ayn and al-wad' are relative accidents yet motion occurs in them directly. Then he answers that non-independence does not preclude motion; it only means they are not independent substrates.
This objection shows the subtlety of the argument—non-independence does not equal impossibility of motion.
5
Reflect on the generalization
Sanusi says the same argument applies to all relative accidents (al-a'rad al-nisbiyya). Think about why this principle is important for understanding categories in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان متبوعها قابلاً للأشد والأضعف (قبلهما المضاف أيضاً ( وإلا فلا) يعني الإضافة تابعة لمعروضها في الحركة بل في التغير مطلقاً لأنها لو تغيرت بلا تغير في معروضها لكانت مستقلة بالمفهومية وعلى هذا فإن كانت الإضافة عارضة لإحدى المقولات الأربع وقعت الحركة فيها تبعاً لها كما إذا فرض أن ماء أشد سخونة من ماء آخر وتحرك في الكيف حتى صار سخونته أضعف من سخونة الآخر، فإن هذا الماء قد انتقل من نوع من الإضافة أعني الأشدية إلى نوع آخر منها أعني الأضعفية انتقالاً تدريجياً، فقد تحرك الجسم في الإضافة تبعا لحركته في معروضها الحقيقي أعني السخونة التي هي من الكيف وكذلك إذا كان الجسم في مكان أعلى ثم تحرك في الأين حتى صار في مكان أسفل، أو كان أصغر مقداراً من جسم آخر، ثم تحرك في الكم حتى صار أعظم مقداراً منه، أو كان على أشرف أوضاعه ثم تحرك منه إلى وضع أخس أوضاعه فقد انتقل الجسم في هذه الصور أيضاً من إضافة إلى أخرى تدريجياً وتبعاً لحركته في معروضها، وكما لا يتصور بقاء هذه الإضافات بأعيانها مع تغير متبوعاتها في أنفسها لا يتصور أيضاً انتقال الجسم وتغيره في هذه الإضافات مع بقاء متبوعاتها على حالها لما عرفت من أنها لو تغيرت في أنفسها بلا تغير في معروضها لاستقلت بالمفهومية، وهذا الدليل بعينه جار في سائر الأعراض النسبية لعدم استقلالها بالمفهومية ومنقوض بالأين والوضع فإنهما من الأعراض النسبية مع وقوع الحركة فيهما بلا تبعية شيء، وحينئذ نقول : لم لا يجوز أن ينتقل الموضوع مضاف إلى آخر تدريجاً؟ فإن كونه غير مستقل بالمفهومية لا ينافي ذلك
For if its subject is receptive to the more and the less (the relative also accepts them, otherwise no), meaning that al-idaafa (relation) is dependent on its substrate in motion, rather in change absolutely, because if it changed without change in its substrate, it would be independent in concept. On this basis, if relation is accidental to one of the four categories, motion occurs in it dependently on them. Just as if we suppose that water is hotter than another water and it moves in quality until its heat becomes weaker than the other's heat, then this water has transferred from one kind of relation, namely intensity, to another kind, namely weakness, gradually. So the body moved in relation following its movement in its true substrate, namely heat, which is from the category of quality. Similarly, if a body is in a higher place and then moves in the category of place (al-ayn) until it becomes in a lower place, or was smaller in quantity than another body and then moves in quantity until it becomes greater in quantity than it, or was in its most honorable position and then moves from it to a lower position: in these cases the body also transferred from one relation to another gradually and following its movement in its substrate. And just as the survival of these relations themselves with the change of their subjects in themselves is inconceivable, so too the body's transfer and change in these relations while their subjects remain as they are is inconceivable, because you have known that if they changed in themselves without change in their substrate, they would be independent in concept. This same argument applies to all relative accidents due to their lack of independence in concept. But it is refuted by al-ayn (place) and al-wad' (position), for they are relative accidents while motion occurs in them without following anything. So then we say: why is it not permissible for the subject to transfer from one relative to another gradually? For its non-independence in concept does not contradict that.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of motion in the category of relation (al-idaafa). It begins by stating the principle: relation is not an independent category; it depends on its substrate (matbū') for its existence. Therefore, motion in relation can only occur if the substrate itself undergoes motion. The author then provides examples: water changing temperature, a body moving from a higher to a lower place, a change in relative quantity, and a change in relative position. In each case, the relation changes only because the underlying quality, place, quantity, or position changes. He then generalizes that the same reasoning applies to all relative accidents (al-a'rāḍ al-nisbiyya). An objection is raised: the categories of place (al-ayn) and position (al-wad') are also relative, yet they accept motion directly without following another category. The author replies that non-independence in concept does not preclude motion in them; it only means they are not independent substrates themselves. Thus, the objection is answered.

Key Terms
الإضافة
الأشد والأضعف
الأعراض النسبية
الأين
الوضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That non-independence of relation means it cannot undergo motion at all.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that non-independence only means motion in relation is not primary; it can still move dependently on its substrate, as seen with al-ayn and al-wad'.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, motion in the category of relation (al-idaafa) occurs only when what changes?
A) the relation itself changes independently
B) the substrate (the property it is relative to) changes
C) the mind conceives it
D) both relata change simultaneously

Key Takeaway
Relation cannot change on its own; its motion is always a reflection of change in its substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often compare yourself to others—smarter, richer, more pious. According to this lesson, such relational attributes are not independent; they follow real changes in you. When you feel superior in some way, does that feeling rest on actual improvement in your character or knowledge, or are you mistaking a relational shift for real growth? What specific step will you take today to ensure your inner self is changing for the better, not just your relative standing?
?
Consider a time you changed your physical location or social position hoping it would change who you are. This lesson says that motion in place or position follows the substrate—the body. If you only change your surroundings without changing your heart, have you truly progressed? Where in your life are you seeking external repositioning to avoid internal transformation?
?
The argument in this lesson exposes that relation cannot stand alone—it depends entirely on its substrate. You may think your identity is defined by your relationships (e.g., parent, spouse, leader), but these are relative accidents. If your substrate (your soul, your faith) remains stagnant, are your relational roles just empty labels? What will you do before the end of this week to strengthen the substance of your soul?

5 min
Relation as a Dependent Category
Definition of relation's non-independence and its implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'non-independent in concept' and why relation cannot have essential rulings.

Core Idea
Relation only exists by inhering in another category.

The commentator begins by defining what it means for relation to be non-independent in concept. This definition is crucial for understanding why relation cannot be a substrate for opposition, motion, or other rulings. Let's examine this definition closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition of 'non-independent in concept' and its literal translation. Notice the emphasis on inherence in another category.
2
Understand the consequence
The commentator explains that because relation inheres in another category, it cannot have any ruling essentially. Reflect on why this is a logical necessity.
The 'least degree of a substrate' requires independence in that substrate-hood.
3
Connect to the overall argument
This definition will be used later to reconcile Ibn Sina's statements. For now, ensure you grasp the core idea.
This is the foundation of the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( غير مستقلة بالمفهومية) أي لا يعقل إلا عارضة لمقولة أخرى، فالعروض المقولة أخرى معتبر في ذاتها فلا يعرض لها حكم من الأحكام بالذات فضلاً عن الحركة لأن أقل درجات المعروض أن يكون مستقلاً بتلك المعروضية فارتفع النقض والمنع قوله الآتي في الشفاء أن التضاد لا يعرض الإضافة فإن الإضافات طبائع مستقلة بأنفسها فيمتنع أن يعرض لها التضاد، لأن أقل درجات المعروض أن يكون مستقلاً بتلك المعروضية فأما كون الآخر ضداً لا براد كالحار والبارد فلأن الإضافات لما كانت طبيعة غير مستقلة بل تابعة لمعروضها وجب أن تكون في الحكم أيضاً تابعة، وإلا لكانت مستقلة فيه هذا لكنه في طبيعياته، وأما مقولة المضاف فبينه أن يكون الانتقال فيها أي هو من حال إلى حال دفعة وإن اختلف في بعض المواضع فيكون التغيير في الحقيقة واقعاً في مقولة أخرى عرضت لها الإضافة، والإضافة من شأنها أن تلحق مقولات أخرى ولا يتحقق بذاتها فإذا كانت المقولة مما يقبل الأشد والأضعف عرض للإضافة مثل ذلك فبين كلاميه تدافع فإن الأول أن لا يقع الانتقال مطلقاً في الإضافة إلا تبعاً لمقولة أخرى والثاني يدل على الانتقال، ولو نفي فيها بالذات يعدم كونها عارضة للموضوع بتوسط مقولة أخرى وإذا كانت عارضة بتوسط مقولة أخرى كانت الانتقال فيها بتبع تلك المقولة، فالحكم بأنها غير مستقلة حكم أكثري لأن عروضها بتوسط المقولات أكثر
His saying: 'non-independent in concept' means that it is not conceivable except as accidental to another category. So the inherence in another category is considered in its essence, so no ruling applies to it essentially, let alone motion, because the least degree of a substrate is that it is independent in that substrate-hood. So the objection and prevention are removed by his upcoming statement in al-Shifa that opposition does not occur to relation, for relations are independent natures in themselves, so it is impossible for opposition to occur to them, because the least degree of a substrate is that it is independent in that substrate-hood. As for the other being a 'opposite without intention' like hot and cold, it is because relations, since they are non-independent in nature but dependent on their substrate, must be dependent also in ruling; otherwise they would be independent in it. This, however, is in his natural philosophy. As for the category of the relative, it is evident that transition in it, i.e., from state to state, is instantaneous, although it differs in some places. So the change in reality occurs in another category to which relation attaches. And relation is such that it attaches to other categories and is not actualized by itself. So if the category accepts the more and the less, relation similarly receives that. Thus, between his two statements there is apparent contradiction: the first indicates that transition does not occur at all in relation except following another category, and the second indicates transition. And if it is denied essentially, then it would not be accidental to the subject through the mediation of another category. And if it is accidental through mediation of another category, then transition in it follows that category. So the judgment that it is non-independent is a general judgment (ḥukm aktharī) because its being accidental through mediation of categories is more common.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'non-independent in concept' (ghayr mustaqilla bil-mafhūmiyya). It explains that relation cannot be conceived except as inhering in another category for its subsistence. The commentator then addresses an apparent contradiction in Ibn Sina's works: in al-Shifa, Ibn Sina says that opposition (al-tadādd) does not apply to relation because relations are independent natures, while elsewhere he treats relation as dependent. The commentary resolves this by stating that the non-independence of relation is a general judgment (ḥukm aktharī), meaning it usually occurs through mediating categories, but not absolutely. Thus, the two statements are consistent: relation is generally dependent, but in some contexts (like opposition) it can be considered independently. The passage also discusses that transition in relation can be instantaneous if the underlying category changes instantaneously, but gradual if the underlying category changes gradually.

Key Terms
غير مستقلة بالمفهومية
عارضة لمقولة أخرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can relation not have any ruling essentially?
A) Because it is a simple concept
B) Because it inheres in another category
C) Because it is not a real entity
D) Because it is a mental construct

Key Takeaway
Relation is a dependent category that cannot be a substrate for essential rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Reconciling Ibn Sina's Statements on Relation
Reconciliation of Ibn Sina's statements via ḥukm aktharī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves the apparent contradiction between Ibn Sina's two statements on the independence of relation.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's statements are consistent via 'general judgment' (ḥukm aktharī).

Ibn Sina appears to contradict himself: in al-Shifa he says relations are independent natures that cannot have opposition, while elsewhere he allows transition in relation. The commentator shows how these two positions are actually compatible by introducing the concept of a general (but not absolute) judgment. Let's follow the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two statements
First, locate Ibn Sina's statement in al-Shifa that opposition does not occur to relation because relations are independent natures. Then, note the second statement that allows transition in relation.
The apparent contradiction is between independence and dependence.
2
Understand the commentator's resolution
The commentator argues that Ibn Sina's first statement is about the nature of relation itself (it is non-independent), while the second is about its accidental rulings. The judgment of non-independence is a 'general judgment' (ḥukm aktharī), not absolute.
This means relation is usually dependent, but can be considered independent in specific contexts.
3
Apply the resolution
Consider the example of opposition: even though relation generally depends on a substrate, when discussing opposition in natural philosophy, Ibn Sina treats relation as independent because the opposition is about the relation itself, not its substrate.
This shows the statements are not contradictory but address different aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( غير مستقلة بالمفهومية) أي لا يعقل إلا عارضة لمقولة أخرى، فالعروض المقولة أخرى معتبر في ذاتها فلا يعرض لها حكم من الأحكام بالذات فضلاً عن الحركة لأن أقل درجات المعروض أن يكون مستقلاً بتلك المعروضية فارتفع النقض والمنع قوله الآتي في الشفاء أن التضاد لا يعرض الإضافة فإن الإضافات طبائع مستقلة بأنفسها فيمتنع أن يعرض لها التضاد، لأن أقل درجات المعروض أن يكون مستقلاً بتلك المعروضية فأما كون الآخر ضداً لا براد كالحار والبارد فلأن الإضافات لما كانت طبيعة غير مستقلة بل تابعة لمعروضها وجب أن تكون في الحكم أيضاً تابعة، وإلا لكانت مستقلة فيه هذا لكنه في طبيعياته، وأما مقولة المضاف فبينه أن يكون الانتقال فيها أي هو من حال إلى حال دفعة وإن اختلف في بعض المواضع فيكون التغيير في الحقيقة واقعاً في مقولة أخرى عرضت لها الإضافة، والإضافة من شأنها أن تلحق مقولات أخرى ولا يتحقق بذاتها فإذا كانت المقولة مما يقبل الأشد والأضعف عرض للإضافة مثل ذلك فبين كلاميه تدافع فإن الأول أن لا يقع الانتقال مطلقاً في الإضافة إلا تبعاً لمقولة أخرى والثاني يدل على الانتقال، ولو نفي فيها بالذات يعدم كونها عارضة للموضوع بتوسط مقولة أخرى وإذا كانت عارضة بتوسط مقولة أخرى كانت الانتقال فيها بتبع تلك المقولة، فالحكم بأنها غير مستقلة حكم أكثري لأن عروضها بتوسط المقولات أكثر
His saying: 'non-independent in concept' means that it is not conceivable except as accidental to another category. So the inherence in another category is considered in its essence, so no ruling applies to it essentially, let alone motion, because the least degree of a substrate is that it is independent in that substrate-hood. So the objection and prevention are removed by his upcoming statement in al-Shifa that opposition does not occur to relation, for relations are independent natures in themselves, so it is impossible for opposition to occur to them, because the least degree of a substrate is that it is independent in that substrate-hood. As for the other being a 'opposite without intention' like hot and cold, it is because relations, since they are non-independent in nature but dependent on their substrate, must be dependent also in ruling; otherwise they would be independent in it. This, however, is in his natural philosophy. As for the category of the relative, it is evident that transition in it, i.e., from state to state, is instantaneous, although it differs in some places. So the change in reality occurs in another category to which relation attaches. And relation is such that it attaches to other categories and is not actualized by itself. So if the category accepts the more and the less, relation similarly receives that. Thus, between his two statements there is apparent contradiction: the first indicates that transition does not occur at all in relation except following another category, and the second indicates transition. And if it is denied essentially, then it would not be accidental to the subject through the mediation of another category. And if it is accidental through mediation of another category, then transition in it follows that category. So the judgment that it is non-independent is a general judgment (ḥukm aktharī) because its being accidental through mediation of categories is more common.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'non-independent in concept' (ghayr mustaqilla bil-mafhūmiyya). It explains that relation cannot be conceived except as inhering in another category for its subsistence. The commentator then addresses an apparent contradiction in Ibn Sina's works: in al-Shifa, Ibn Sina says that opposition (al-tadādd) does not apply to relation because relations are independent natures, while elsewhere he treats relation as dependent. The commentary resolves this by stating that the non-independence of relation is a general judgment (ḥukm aktharī), meaning it usually occurs through mediating categories, but not absolutely. Thus, the two statements are consistent: relation is generally dependent, but in some contexts (like opposition) it can be considered independently. The passage also discusses that transition in relation can be instantaneous if the underlying category changes instantaneously, but gradual if the underlying category changes gradually.

The Category of Time (Mata) and Its Dependence on Motion
Sanusi discusses Ibn Sina's statement and objection on time and motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's assertion that time exists for a body only through motion, and how this view refutes the objection that time could be a subject of motion.

Core Idea
Time is a measure of motion, not a container.

This lesson explores a critical aspect of the category of time (mata) in Islamic philosophy. Ibn Sina argues that time is not an independent substance but is intimately tied to motion. We will examine his reasoning and the objection that time itself might undergo motion, and see how he repels it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to how Ibn Sina's claim is presented and the objection that follows.
The passage contains dense philosophical reasoning; read slowly.
2
Identify the main claim and objection
Ibn Sina states that time exists for a body through motion, and that a motionless body has no time. The objection suggests that time could be established through one motion while another motion occurs in it.
The objection challenges the necessary connection between time and motion.
3
Follow the refutation
The commentary explains that if motion occurred in time, then time would require another time, leading to an infinite regress. This shows time cannot be a subject of motion.
The refutation relies on the principle that every motion is in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما متى؟ فقال: ( ابن سينا ( في النجاة إن وجوده لجسم يتبع الحركة ) أي ثبوته له بتوسط الحركة، فإن ما لا حركة فيه ولا تغير لم يتصور له متى ) فكيف تقع فيه الحركة ؟ ) إذ لو وقعت فيه لم يكن تابعاً لها واعترض عليه بأنه يجوز أن يكون ثبوته للجسم بتوسط نوع من الحركة ويقع فيه نوع آخر منها، وفي النجاة أيضاً أن كل حركة فهي في متى فلو كان في متى حركة لكان لمتى متى آخر وهو محال إذ يلزم أن يكون للزمان معا قوله : (إن وجوده للجسم خلاصته أن متى كل جسم تابع لحركته في الوجود، وإذا ما لا حركة له متى له فلو وقع حركة بجسم في متى كانت تلك الحركة تابعة لمتاه وغير متفرعة على وجوده لكونه مسافة لها ولا يكون تلك الحركة متنوعة لمتى آخر ولا يكون بجسم واحد متيان وحينئذ اندفع الاعتراض، كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( كل حركة فهي في متى إلخ) يعني أنَّ لها متى لأنَّ كل حركة حاصلة في زمان فلو كان في متى حركة لكان للمتى الذي فرض مسافة الحركة متى آخر عارضة للحركة الواقعة فيه أي يكون حصول هذه الحركة في زمان واقعاً في حصول شيء ما في زمان فيكون للزمان زمان لأنَّ الزمان الثاني تابع للحركة الواقعة في متى يزيد بزيادتها وينتقص بانتقاصها ويتفاوت بسبب سرعتها وبطئها في الطول والقصر بخلاف زمان المتى الذي وقع الحركة فيه فإنه تابع لحركة الأمر الذي هو متاه، وعلى هذا لا اتجاه للاعتراض المذكور كما لا يخفى
As for mata (when/time)? He said: Ibn Sina in al-Najat: 'Its existence for a body follows motion' i.e., its establishment for it is through motion. For what has no motion and no change, no time can be conceived for it. So how can motion occur in it? For if motion occurred in it, it would not be dependent on it. And an objection was raised against it: it is permissible that its establishment for the body is through one kind of motion and another kind of motion occurs in it. And in al-Najat also: 'Every motion is in time'. So if there were motion in time, then time would have another time, which is impossible, because then time would have time together. His saying: 'His saying: (Its existence for the body) — its summary is that the time of every body follows its motion in existence. And if something has no motion, it has no time. So if motion occurs in a body in time, that motion is dependent on its time and not derived from its existence because it is a distance for it. And that motion is not of a different kind for another time, nor does one body have two times. Then the objection is repelled, as is not hidden. His saying: (Every motion is in time, etc.) means that it has time because every motion occurs in time. So if there were motion in time, then the time that was hypothesized as the distance of motion would have another time accidental to the motion occurring in it, i.e., the occurrence of this motion in time would occur in the occurrence of something in time, so time would have time. Because the second time is dependent on the motion occurring in time, increasing with its increase and decreasing with its decrease, and varying in length and shortness due to its speed and slowness, unlike the time of the time in which the motion occurred, for it is dependent on the motion of the thing that is its time. On this basis, the mentioned objection has no direction, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage addresses the category of time (mata). It presents Ibn Sina's view from al-Najat that time exists for a body only through motion; a body without motion has no time. An objection is raised: perhaps time is established through one kind of motion while another kind of motion occurs in it. The author then cites another statement from al-Najat: 'Every motion is in time.' If motion were in time (i.e., if time itself were a subject of motion), then that time would require another time, leading to an infinite regress. The commentary explains that the time of a body is dependent on its motion, so motion occurring in that time does not require a second time; rather, the time is the measure of the motion. The passage concludes that this reasoning defeats the objection.

الزمان
الحركة
النجاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time can be a subject of motion itself.
Correction: Time is a measure of motion; motion cannot occur in time in a way that makes time a subject, as that would lead to an infinite regress.

Checkpoint

A) Time is independent of motion
B) Time's existence for a body is through motion
C) Motion occurs in time but time is not dependent on motion
D) Time and motion are identical

Key Takeaway
Time is dependent on motion; without motion, time is not conceivable.

The True Meaning of Non-Independence and the Reply to the Objection from Al-Ayn and Al-Wad'
The commentator refines the answer to the objection that al-ayn and al-wad' are relative yet accept motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'non-independence of relation' in kalam and how it avoids the objection from place and position.

Core Idea
Non-independence is not mere relativity but dependence on substrates in rulings.

In the ongoing discussion about whether relations (iḍāfāt) are independent, an objection was raised using the categories of place (al-ayn) and position (al-wad'), which are relative yet admit motion without a substrate. The commentator clarifies that 'non-independence' has a more precise meaning that deflects this objection. Let us examine the text to understand this refined definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Notice the compressed style and the key terms.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two definitions of non-independence
The passage contrasts a mere relative (nisbiyya) understanding with the correct understanding: dependence on substrates in rulings (aḥkām). Highlight these two meanings.
3
Understand the objection and its refutation
The objection claimed that al-ayn and al-wad' refute non-independence because they are relative yet accept motion. The refutation redefines non-independence so that the objection no longer applies.
Ask yourself: why does the refined definition avoid the objection?
4
Reflect on the broader significance
This clarification is crucial for the theory of relations. It shows that non-independence is not a simple property but a specific kind of dependence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومنقوض بالأين (والوضع وقد يجاب أن ليس معنى عدم استقلال الإضافة مجرد كونها نسبية حتى ينتقض بالأين والوضع بل معناه كونها تابعة لمعروضاتها في الأحكام وأنت خبير بأن الكلام في ثبوت هذه التبعية
His saying: 'And it is refuted by al-ayn and al-wad'.' It may be answered that the meaning of non-independence of relation is not merely its being relative such that it is refuted by al-ayn and al-wad'; rather, its meaning is that it is dependent on its substrates in rulings (aḥkām). And you are well aware that the discussion is about the establishment of this dependence.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This brief passage provides a refined answer to the earlier objection that the categories of place (al-ayn) and position (al-wad') are relative yet motion occurs in them without following another substrate. The commentator clarifies that 'non-independence' (ʿadam al-istiqlāl) of relation does not mean merely that it is relative (nisbiyya); rather, it means that relation is dependent on its substrates specifically in terms of rulings (aḥkām). In other words, relation's properties and judgments follow those of its substrate. This definition avoids the objection because al-ayn and al-wad', while relative, are not dependent on another substrate for their rulings in the same way. Thus, the objection does not apply.

The Dependence of Relative Categories on Their Subjects
Illustrates dependence of relative categories on their subjects through examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why relative categories (al-i'idafat) cannot change independently of their subjects and how examples like heat, place, quantity, and posture illustrate this dependence.

Core Idea
Relative categories change only with their subjects, never alone.

This lesson explores a key principle in kalam physics: relative categories (such as heat, place, quantity, and posture) do not have independent existence—they are always attached to a subject. The passage uses concrete examples to show how these categories change gradually as their underlying body changes, and argues that if they could change alone, they would be independently conceivable, which they are not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage examples
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the four examples: heat, motion in place, quantity, and posture. Notice how each change follows the body's motion.
The examples show the body moving from one relative state to another gradually.
2
Identify the principle
Find the sentence that states the general rule: just as relations cannot persist when subjects change, the body cannot change in relations while subjects stay the same.
The reason given is that relative categories are not independently conceivable.
3
Understand the extension
Notice that the same proof applies to all relative accidents (a'rad nisbiyya). This makes the principle universal for this type of category.
4
Reflect on the key term
Focus on the term 'mustaqilla bil-mafhumiyya' (independently conceivable). Why does lack of this force dependence on a subject?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السخونة التي هي من الكيف وكذلك إذا كان جسم في مكان أعلى ثم تحرك في الأين حتى صار في مكان أسفل، أو كان أصغر مقداراً من جسم آخر، ثم تحرك في الكم حتى صار أعظم مقداراً منه، أو كان على أشرف أوضاعه ثم تحرك منه إلى وضع أخس أوضاعه فقد انتقل الجسم في هذه الصور أيضاً من إضافة إلى أخرى تدريجياً وتبعاً لحركته في معروضها، وكما لا يتصور بقاء هذه الإضافات بأعيانها مع تغير متبوعاتها في أنفسها لا يتصور أيضاً انتقال الجسم وتغيره في هذه الإضافات مع بقاء متبوعاتها على حالها لما عرفت من أنها لو تغيرت في أنفسها بلا تغير في معروضها لاستقلت بالمفهومية، وهذا الدليل بعينه جار في سائر الأعراض النسبية لعدم استقلالها بالمفهومية ومنقوض بالأين والوضع فإنهما من الأعراض النسبية مع وقوع الحركة فيهما بلا تبعية شيء، وحينئذ نقول : لم لا يجوز أن ينتقل الموضوع مضاف إلى آخر تدريجاً؟ فإن كونه غير مستقل بالمفهومية لا ينافي ذلك
Heat, which is from quality, and likewise when a body is in a higher place, then it moves in 'where' (al-ayn) until it becomes in a lower place, or it was of a smaller quantity than another body, then it moves in quantity (al-kamm) until it becomes of a greater quantity than it, or it was in its most honorable posture (al-wad'), then it moves from it to a lowlier posture, then the body has also transferred in these forms from one relation (i'idafa) to another gradually and following its motion in their subject (ma'rud). And just as the persistence of these relations in their very selves cannot be conceived with the change of their subjects (matbu'at) in themselves, likewise the transfer and change of the body in these relations with the persistence of their subjects in their state cannot be conceived, because you have known that if they changed in themselves without change in their subject, they would be independently conceivable. And this same proof applies to all relative accidents (al-a'rad al-nisbiyya) due to their lack of independent conceivability. And it is refuted by 'where' (al-ayn) and 'posture' (al-wad'), for they are among the relative accidents despite the occurrence of motion in them without following anything. Then we say: why is it not permissible that the subject transfers from one relation to another gradually? For its being not independently conceivable does not contradict that.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of motion in relative categories (al-i'idafat). It begins by giving examples: heat (a quality) moves with the body, motion in 'ayn (where) when a body moves from high to low, motion in kam (quantity) when a body becomes larger, and motion in wad' (posture) when a body changes position. In all these cases, the body moves gradually from one relative state to another, following the motion in the subject. The passage then states the principle: just as these relations cannot persist when their subjects change, so too the body cannot change in these relations while the subjects remain unchanged. Why? Because if they changed independently, they would be independently conceivable (mustaqilla bil-mafhumiyya), but they are not. The same proof applies to all relative accidents (al-a'rad al-nisbiyya) because they lack independent conceivability. An objection is then raised: the categories of 'ayn (where) and wad' (posture) are also relative, yet motion occurs in them without following something else. The response: even these are not independent; the fact that they are not independently conceivable does not prevent them from being subjects of motion. In fact, the subject can move gradually from one relation to another; not being independently conceivable does not negate that.

Key Terms
إضافة (i'idafa)
أعراض نسبية (a'rad nisbiyya)
متبوع (matbu')
معروض (ma'rud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think relative categories like 'place' or 'posture' can change by themselves without the body moving.
Correction: The passage argues that these are relative and depend on a subject; they change only when the subject changes, as illustrated by the examples.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do relative categories change only when their subjects change?
A) Because they are substances.
B) Because they are not independently conceivable.
C) Because motion only occurs in non-relative categories.
D) Because the subject is the only thing that can change.

Key Takeaway
Relative categories are not independently conceivable; they depend on their subjects and change only when those subjects change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you try to change a relational attribute (like your status or comfort) while ignoring the need for change in yourself (the subject)? Reflect on one such area and commit to changing the subject, not just the appearance.
?
The passage shows that relative categories cannot persist when the subject changes. Consider your relationship with Allah: do you treat your good deeds as independent 'relations' that can stay the same even when your heart changes? How will you ensure your inner state matches your outward actions?
?
The proof that relative accidents lack independent conceivability reminds us that nothing in creation stands alone. In what way do you still try to live as if you are self-sufficient? What would it mean for you to fully acknowledge your dependence on Allah in a specific aspect of your life today?

The Objection from 'Ayn and Wad' and Its Resolution
Presents objection that 'ayn and wad are relative yet move independently, and responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that 'where' (al-ayn) and 'posture' (al-wad') are relative yet undergo independent motion is invalid, and how the response defends the principle of dependence.

Core Idea
Even 'where' and 'posture' are relative; their motion does not break the rule.

The previous lesson established that relative categories depend on their subjects and cannot change alone. However, an observant student might object: 'where' (al-ayn) and 'posture' (al-wad') are relative, but a body can change place or posture without any change in its substance—doesn't that contradict the rule? This lesson presents that objection and the author's response, showing that even these categories are not independent and that change in them still follows the subject gradually.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the part where it says the proof is 'refuted' (manqud) by 'ayn and wad'. Understand why someone would see these as counterexamples.
The objector says motion occurs in 'ayn and wad without following something else.
2
Analyze the response
The author replies: 'Why is it not permissible that the subject transfers from one relation to another gradually?' Note that he does not deny the motion, but reinterprets it.
He argues that lack of independent conceivability does not prevent gradual transition.
3
Connect to the principle
See how the response maintains the original principle: even in 'ayn and wad, the change is not independent; it follows the subject's motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السخونة التي هي من الكيف وكذلك إذا كان جسم في مكان أعلى ثم تحرك في الأين حتى صار في مكان أسفل، أو كان أصغر مقداراً من جسم آخر، ثم تحرك في الكم حتى صار أعظم مقداراً منه، أو كان على أشرف أوضاعه ثم تحرك منه إلى وضع أخس أوضاعه فقد انتقل الجسم في هذه الصور أيضاً من إضافة إلى أخرى تدريجياً وتبعاً لحركته في معروضها، وكما لا يتصور بقاء هذه الإضافات بأعيانها مع تغير متبوعاتها في أنفسها لا يتصور أيضاً انتقال الجسم وتغيره في هذه الإضافات مع بقاء متبوعاتها على حالها لما عرفت من أنها لو تغيرت في أنفسها بلا تغير في معروضها لاستقلت بالمفهومية، وهذا الدليل بعينه جار في سائر الأعراض النسبية لعدم استقلالها بالمفهومية ومنقوض بالأين والوضع فإنهما من الأعراض النسبية مع وقوع الحركة فيهما بلا تبعية شيء، وحينئذ نقول : لم لا يجوز أن ينتقل الموضوع مضاف إلى آخر تدريجاً؟ فإن كونه غير مستقل بالمفهومية لا ينافي ذلك
Heat, which is from quality, and likewise when a body is in a higher place, then it moves in 'where' (al-ayn) until it becomes in a lower place, or it was of a smaller quantity than another body, then it moves in quantity (al-kamm) until it becomes of a greater quantity than it, or it was in its most honorable posture (al-wad'), then it moves from it to a lowlier posture, then the body has also transferred in these forms from one relation (i'idafa) to another gradually and following its motion in their subject (ma'rud). And just as the persistence of these relations in their very selves cannot be conceived with the change of their subjects (matbu'at) in themselves, likewise the transfer and change of the body in these relations with the persistence of their subjects in their state cannot be conceived, because you have known that if they changed in themselves without change in their subject, they would be independently conceivable. And this same proof applies to all relative accidents (al-a'rad al-nisbiyya) due to their lack of independent conceivability. And it is refuted by 'where' (al-ayn) and 'posture' (al-wad'), for they are among the relative accidents despite the occurrence of motion in them without following anything. Then we say: why is it not permissible that the subject transfers from one relation to another gradually? For its being not independently conceivable does not contradict that.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of motion in relative categories (al-i'idafat). It begins by giving examples: heat (a quality) moves with the body, motion in 'ayn (where) when a body moves from high to low, motion in kam (quantity) when a body becomes larger, and motion in wad' (posture) when a body changes position. In all these cases, the body moves gradually from one relative state to another, following the motion in the subject. The passage then states the principle: just as these relations cannot persist when their subjects change, so too the body cannot change in these relations while the subjects remain unchanged. Why? Because if they changed independently, they would be independently conceivable (mustaqilla bil-mafhumiyya), but they are not. The same proof applies to all relative accidents (al-a'rad al-nisbiyya) because they lack independent conceivability. An objection is then raised: the categories of 'ayn (where) and wad' (posture) are also relative, yet motion occurs in them without following something else. The response: even these are not independent; the fact that they are not independently conceivable does not prevent them from being subjects of motion. In fact, the subject can move gradually from one relation to another; not being independently conceivable does not negate that.

وضع (wad')
إضافة (i'idafa)
أعراض نسبية (a'rad nisbiyya)

Key Takeaway
Even in 'where' and 'posture', change is not independent; it is the subject moving gradually from one relation to another.

Ibn Sina on the Dependence of 'Mata' on Motion
Ibn Sina's argument on 'mata' dependence on motion and refutation of motion in 'mata'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that the category of 'mata' (when) depends on motion and cannot itself be a locus for motion without infinite regress.

Core Idea
'Mata' follows motion; it cannot contain motion without spawning another 'mata'.

Ibn Sina in al-Najat uses the category of 'mata' (when) to show its dependence on motion. He argues that if something has no motion, it has no 'mata'. But then he faces a question: can motion occur in 'mata'? His answer draws on the principle that every motion is already in a 'mata', leading to an infinite regress if 'mata' itself contained motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Ibn Sina's core claim and the objection.
Pay attention to the distinction between 'mata' as derived from motion and the possibility of motion occurring in 'mata'.
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
Break the passage into (1) Ibn Sina's claim, (2) the objection, (3) his rebuttal using infinite regress.
The rebuttal is the key: every motion is in 'mata', so if 'mata' contained motion, that motion would need another 'mata'.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument matters for understanding time and motion in medieval philosophy.
It shows that time is not an independent container but a derivative of motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وأما متى ؟ فقال : ( ابن سينا ( في النجاة إن وجوده لجسم يتبع الحركة ) أي ثبوته له بتوسط الحركة، فإن ما لا حركة فيه ولا تغير لم يتصور له متى ) فكيف تقع فيه الحركة ؟ ) إذ لو وقعت فيه لم يكن تابعاً لها واعترض عليه بأنه يجوز أن يكون ثبوته للجسم بتوسط نوع من الحركة ويقع فيه نوع آخر منها، وفي النجاة أيضاً أن كل حركة فهي في متى فلو كان في متى حركة لكان لمتى متى آخر وهو محال إذ يلزم أن يكون للزمان
قوله : (إن وجوده للجسم خلاصته أن متى كل جسم تابع لحركته في الوجود، وإذا ما لا حركة له متى له فلو وقع حركة بجسم في متى كانت تلك الحركة تابعة لمتاه وغير متفرعة على وجوده لكونه مسافة لها ولا يكون تلك الحركة متنوعة لمتى آخر ولا يكون بجسم واحد متيان وحينئذ اندفع الاعتراض، كما لا يخفى .
قوله : ( كل حركة فهي في متى إلخ) يعني أنَّ لها متى لأنَّ كل حركة حاصلة في زمان فلو كان في متى حركة لكان للمتى الذي فرض مسافة الحركة متى آخر عارضة للحركة الواقعة فيه أي يكون حصول هذه الحركة في زمان واقعاً في حصول شيء ما في زمان فيكون للزمان زمان لأنَّ الزمان الثاني تابع للحركة الواقعة في متى يزيد بزيادتها وينتقص بانتقاصها ويتفاوت بسبب سرعتها وبطئها في الطول والقصر بخلاف زمان المتى الذي وقع الحركة فيه فإنه تابع لحركة الأمر الذي هو متاه، وعلى هذا لا اتجاه للاعتراض المذكور كما لا يخفى .
(And as for 'when' (mata)? He (Ibn Sina) said in al-Najat: 'Its existence for a body follows motion') i.e., its establishment for it by means of motion. For what has no motion and no change, 'when' cannot be conceived for it. So how can motion occur in it? Because if it occurred in it, it would not be a follower of it. And it was objected against him that it is permissible that its establishment for the body be by means of one type of motion, and another type of motion occurs in it. And in al-Najat also: 'Every motion is in 'when'. So if there were motion in 'when', then 'when' would have another 'when', which is impossible, for it would entail that time has time.'
His saying: ('Its existence for the body') its summary is that the 'when' of every body follows its motion in existence, and what has no motion has no 'when' for it. So if motion were to occur in a body's 'when', that motion would be subordinate to its 'when' and not derived from its existence, because it is a distance for it. And that motion would not be varied for another 'when', and there would not be two 'when's for one body. Then the objection is repelled, as is not hidden.
His saying: ('Every motion is in 'when' etc.') means that it has a 'when' because every motion occurs in time. So if there were motion in 'when', then the 'when' which is assumed as the distance of motion would have another 'when' supervening on the motion occurring in it. That is, the occurrence of this motion in time would be an occurrence of something in time, so time would have time, because the second time follows the motion occurring in 'when', increasing with its increase, decreasing with its decrease, and varying in length and shortness due to its speed and slowness, unlike the time of the 'when' in which the motion occurred, for it follows the motion of the thing which is its 'when'. On this basis, there is no direction for the mentioned objection, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's argument from al-Najat regarding the category of 'mata' (when). He states that the existence of 'mata' for a body follows motion; if there is no motion or change, then 'mata' cannot be conceived for that body. Therefore, motion cannot occur in 'mata' itself, because if it did, it would not be following it. An objection is raised: perhaps 'mata' is established in the body through one type of motion and another type of motion occurs in it. Ibn Sina also adds in al-Najat that every motion is in 'mata' (i.e., has a time), so if there were motion in 'mata', then that 'mata' would require another 'mata', which is absurd because it would require a time for time. The commentary then explains: the summary is that 'mata' of every body is dependent on its motion in existence; anything without motion has no 'mata'. So if motion were to occur in a body's 'mata', that motion would be subordinate to its 'mata' and not derived from its existence. And there cannot be two different 'mata' for one body. The second commentary explains that if every motion is in 'mata', and we suppose motion in 'mata', then that 'mata' would be the distance for the motion, and that motion would have another 'mata', leading to an infinite regress of times. Therefore, the objection is invalid.

زمان
تبعية
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'mata' is an independent container for motion, like a vessel.
Correction: Ibn Sina shows that 'mata' is derived from motion and cannot itself contain motion without an infinite regress of times.

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Sina argue that motion cannot occur in the category of 'mata'?

B) Because if motion occurred in 'mata', then 'mata' would require another 'mata', leading to an infinite regress.
C) Because motion is only in quantity.
D) Because 'mata' is a substance.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's argument shows that time ('mata') is dependent on motion and cannot be a container for motion without paradox.

Instantaneous and Gradual Transition in 'When'
Al-Shifa discusses instantaneous transition due to time continuity and the objection regarding gradual transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two types of transition in the category of 'when' (mata): instantaneous due to time's continuity and gradual when there is a time interval.

Core Idea
The 'now' makes transitions instantaneous; intervals make them gradual.

In this passage, al-Shifa examines whether transition in the category of 'when' (mata) is instantaneous or gradual. He argues that because time is a continuum with the 'now' as a common boundary, some transitions—like from year to year—occur at once. However, the objection notes that when two times are separated by a period, the transition is gradual. This lesson unpacks this nuanced discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the instantaneous argument
Read the Arabic and translation for the claim that transition in 'mata' is instantaneous because time parts are continuous and share the now as boundary.
Notice the example of year to year.
2
Analyze the objection
Read the objection using the analogy of distance: when there is a divisible distance, transition is gradual; similarly, when there is a time interval, transition becomes gradual.
Contrast the two scenarios: contiguous vs. separated.
3
Synthesize the two cases
Reflect on the conclusion: transitions in 'mata' can be instantaneous when times are contiguous, but gradual when a time interval exists.

Time, and it was objected that it is permissible for 'when' (mata) to be an accident of time itself, not for another time, like the occurrence of 'before-ness' and 'after-ness'. And he said in al-Shifa: 'It resembles that the transition in 'when' is instantaneous (daf'i), for the transition from year to year and from month to month occurs at once. That is because the parts of time are continuous with one another, and the common boundary between them is the now (al-an). So if two times are assumed to share an instant, then before that instant the subject continues to have its 'when' in relation to the first time, and after it continues to have its 'when' in relation to the second time, and that instant is the end of the existence of the first and the beginning of the occurrence of the second. So there is no gradualness in the transition.' And it is objected against it that the separator between parts of a distance is indivisible boundaries, so the transition from some of those parts to others is instantaneous too. But if two places are assumed with a divisible distance between them, the transition from one to the other is gradual. Similarly, in the transition from a time to another time with a time between them, like dawn and sunset for example, it is gradual too, not instantaneous. Then he said in al-Shifa: 'And it resembles that the state of 'when' is like the state of relation (i'idafa): that the transition in it is dependent on the transition in something else, either quantity or quality. So the change occurs in that thing first, and time is a concomitant (lazim) of that change, so by reason of it change occurs in it.' And he pointed to that by his saying: 'And it (i.e., 'when') is like relation in accepting motion by way of dependence, because it is a relation dependent on its subject. So it is not independently conceivable and changeable.' And you have known what is in it. 'And likewise possession (al-mulk),' for it too is a relative category dependent on its subject in change and stability. 'And as for' the two categories (that...)
His saying: ('Then he said in al-Shifa: It resembles etc.') He intends that the transition occurring in 'when' (mata) is in two ways: one is due to the transition of time and its instability, and that is instantaneous, because time is one continuous thing in itself, and its 'when' by this consideration is one with no multiplicity, let alone transition. And if division is assumed, the common boundary between the two times is the now, so the transition occurring in the things due to the transition of time and its instability is instantaneous. On this basis, the mentioned objection by his saying 'and it is objected against it' etc. is repelled, and it is apparent. The second is the transition due to the change of the things occurring in it, and this transition, since change occurs in it, is momentaneous (ani), so the transition from 'when' is momentaneous even if gradual, then gradual. So the summary of the Shaykh's speech in al-Najat is that motion does not occur in 'when' essentially, and in al-Shifa that the transition occurring in it due to the change of time and its instability is instantaneous, and the transition occurring in it due to the change of things occurring in it follows the change. There is no confusion in the Shaykh's speech, and he is not of two minds about the occurrence of motion in 'when' as the phrasing of the matn suggests and some deficient ones believed. His saying: ('So it is not independently conceivable') i.e., the occurrence of something is not intellectually conceived except after considering change in something, so it is not independent in motion.
His saying: ('And you have known what is in it') You have known its repulsion by what we have written.
His saying: ('And likewise possession etc.') This explanation is incomplete because of the occurrence of the refutation by the prevention mentioned earlier. And in
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage from al-Shifa discusses two ways of transition in 'mata'. First, it suggests that transition in 'mata' might be instantaneous (daf'i) because parts of time are continuous with a common point (al-an, the now). When two times share a now, before that now the subject has one 'mata' relative to the first time, and after it another 'mata' relative to the second time, so there is no gradual transition. An objection is raised: the boundaries between parts of a distance are indivisible, so transition between them is instantaneous, but if there is a divisible distance between two places, transition is gradual. Similarly, between two times with a time interval, transition is gradual. Then al-Shifa says that 'mata' is like a relation (i'idafa): transition in it is dependent on transition in some other category like quantity or quality; the change occurs first, and time (zaman) is a concomitant of that change. The commentary explains that transition in 'mata' can be via two ways: one due to the transition of time and its instability, which is instantaneous because time is one continuous entity; the other due to changes in events within time, which is gradual. The summary is that in al-Najat, motion does not occur in 'mata' essentially, while in al-Shifa, transition due to time's flow is instantaneous, and transition due to changes of events is gradual. Then it discusses 'mulk' (possession) also as a relative category subject to similar analysis, but notes that the explanation there is incomplete due to a refutation mentioned earlier.

Key Terms
متى (mata)
دفعي (daf'i)
تدريجي (tadriji)
زمان (zaman)
آن (an)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All transitions in the category of 'when' are gradual.
Correction: Al-Shifa shows that when two times share an instant, the transition is instantaneous. Gradual transition occurs only when there is a time interval between the two times.

Checkpoint

A) The now is indivisible.
B) There is a time interval between dawn and sunset.
C) The parts of time are continuous.
D) The transition is dependent on quantity.

Key Takeaway
Transition in 'mata' can be instantaneous when times are contiguous, but gradual when a time interval separates them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson distinguishes between instantaneous and gradual transition based on whether times share a 'now'. When you think about a significant change in your life—like a decision to repent or a shift in worldview—do you experience it as an instant or as a process? What does the existence of that 'now' moment mean for your accountability before Allah?
?
Consider the 'now' as a boundary: it is both the end of one time and the start of another. In your spiritual journey, is there a 'now' that you are currently at, a boundary between your past sins and your future obedience? How will you cross that boundary—instantaneously by a single, sincere intention, or gradually by persistent effort?
?
The objection in the passage shows that when there is a gap (a divisible interval) between two times, transition is gradual. Have you ever used the excuse of 'time' to delay a required change, thinking it must be gradual? Does this lesson challenge you to identify moments when an instantaneous shift is possible and required?

The Relational Nature of 'Mata' and Its Dependence on Other Categories
Al-Shifa compares 'mata' to i'idafa and mulk, arguing its transition is dependent on other categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the category of 'when' (mata) is a relative concept whose transition depends on changes in other categories like quantity or quality, similar to relation (i'idafa) and possession (mulk).

Core Idea
Mata is a relative category that follows changes in other categories.

Having considered the instantaneous and gradual possibilities, al-Shifa now likens 'mata' to the category of relation (i'idafa). He argues that transition in 'mata' occurs only as a consequence of change in something else, such as quantity or quality. This lesson explores that relational nature and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the comparison with i'idafa
Read the Arabic and translation to see how al-Shifa compares 'mata' to relation (i'idafa) and possession (mulk).
Notice the phrase 'transition dependent on transition in something else'.
2
Analyze the dependence
Identify that change in 'mata' is not primary but follows change in quantity or quality; time is a concomitant of that change.
3
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how this relational view resolves the earlier puzzle of instantaneous vs. gradual transitions: they are not intrinsic to 'mata' but reflect the underlying change.
Think of examples: the 'when' of an event changes only when the event itself changes.

And he said in al-Shifa: 'It resembles that the transition in 'when' is instantaneous (daf'i), for the transition from year to year and from month to month occurs at once. That is because the parts of time are continuous with one another, and the common boundary between them is the now (al-an). So if two times are assumed to share an instant, then before that instant the subject continues to have its 'when' in relation to the first time, and after it continues to have its 'when' in relation to the second time, and that instant is the end of the existence of the first and the beginning of the occurrence of the second. His saying: ('So it is not independently conceivable') i.e., the occurrence of something is not intellectually conceived except after considering change in something, so it is not independent in motion.

Commentary
This passage from al-Shifa discusses two ways of transition in 'mata'. First, it suggests that transition in 'mata' might be instantaneous (daf'i) because parts of time are continuous with a common point (al-an, the now). When two times share a now, before that now the subject has one 'mata' relative to the first time, and after it another 'mata' relative to the second time, so there is no gradual transition. An objection is raised: the boundaries between parts of a distance are indivisible, so transition between them is instantaneous, but if there is a divisible distance between two places, transition is gradual. Similarly, between two times with a time interval, transition is gradual. Then al-Shifa says that 'mata' is like a relation (i'idafa): transition in it is dependent on transition in some other category like quantity or quality; the change occurs first, and time (zaman) is a concomitant of that change. The commentary explains that transition in 'mata' can be via two ways: one due to the transition of time and its instability, which is instantaneous because time is one continuous entity; the other due to changes in events within time, which is gradual. The summary is that in al-Najat, motion does not occur in 'mata' essentially, while in al-Shifa, transition due to time's flow is instantaneous, and transition due to changes of events is gradual. Then it discusses 'mulk' (possession) also as a relative category subject to similar analysis, but notes that the explanation there is incomplete due to a refutation mentioned earlier.

The Unity of Motion: Personal, Specific, and Generic
Al-Jurjani explains that motion's unity depends on the unity of its six requisites and introduces the three types of unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how motion's unity is categorized into three types and why personal unity requires a single subject.

Core Idea
Motion’s unity mirrors the unity of its six requisites.

This passage introduces the sixth major objective: how motion relates to unity. Al-Jurjani recaps that motion depends on six factors, and its unity is necessarily tied to the unity of those factors. He then classifies the unity of motion into three types: personal, specific, and generic. The first type, personal unity, demands that the subject of motion also be one in person – a principle that grounds the uniqueness of each individual motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three types of unity
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Al-Jurjani states that motion's unity is either personal, specific, or generic.
The three terms are: شخصية, نوعية, جنسية.
2
Focus on personal unity and its requirement
The passage explains that for motion to be one in person, its subject must also be one in person. Understand why this is necessary.
This is because an accident cannot be one in person if it inheres in multiple subjects.
3
Reflect on celestial motion
The comment mentions that celestial motion is eternal, which complicates the idea of unique starting and ending points. Consider how this might affect the unity of such motion.

The Three Conditions of Specific Unity of Motion
Al-Jurjani defines specific unity of motion and gives its three conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of specific unity of motion and its three conditions: unity of substrate, origin, and endpoint.

Core Idea
Specific unity of motion requires only three type-level unities.

In this lesson, Al-Jurjani explains that the specific unity of motion is a simpler concept than personal unity. While personal unity requires many individuating constraints, specific unity only needs the motion's substrate, origin, and endpoint to be of the same type. This distinction clarifies how we classify motions as belonging to the same kind.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the full translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the terms 'ما فيه', 'ما منه', 'ما إليه'.
2
Understand the distinction between personal and specific unity
The author notes that specific unity is part of personal unity. Personal unity requires individuating constraints, while specific unity only requires type-level sameness.
This distinction is key to understanding the simplicity of specific unity.
3
Identify the three conditions
Look for the three conditions listed: unity of what it is in, what it is from, and what it is to. Each refers to the type of substrate, origin, and endpoint.
These three are sufficient for specific unity.
4
Reflect on why only type-level unity matters
Ask yourself: Why are these three conditions enough? Because specific unity only cares about the kind of motion, not the precise individual details.
This connects to the broader classification of motions in kalam.

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the specific unity (الوحدة النوعية) of motion. The author states that the second investigation concerns specific unity. He notes that what is considered for specific unity is part of what is considered for personal unity. The reason is that the individual (person) is the species (type) plus individuating constraints. For specific unity, only three aspects need to be unified in type: what the motion is in (e.g., the kind of substance), what it is from (the starting point or origin), and what it is to (the endpoint). If these three are of the same type, the motion is considered specifically one. This is a simpler condition than personal unity, which requires more specific identification.
