The Unity of the Mover and Specific Difference in Motion
The author refutes the idea that the mover's unity determines motion's specific unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the identity of the mover does not necessitate a difference in the species of motion, and how two motions with different movers can be identical in essence.

Core Idea
The mover's identity does not define the motion's essence; two motions can be identical in essence despite different movers.

This passage tackles a subtle point in kalam physics: whether the cause of motion determines its type. The author argues that a stone's forced upward motion and fire's natural upward motion are the same in essence, despite their different movers. This challenges the assumption that the mover's nature directly shapes the motion's species. Let's work through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the core claim and counter-argument
Start by reading the passage carefully. Notice how the author presents an initial claim that the unity of the mover does not necessitate specific difference in motion, and then considers a counter-argument about principiality and finality.
The counter-argument is introduced with 'قالوا' or similar.
2
Identify the refutation based on species vs individual
The author refutes the counter-argument by using a logical principle: what necessitates difference in species also necessitates difference in the individual, but the converse is not always true. Since difference in mover does not necessitate difference in individual motion, it cannot necessitate difference in species.
3
Examine the concrete example
Focus on the example of the stone's forced upward motion and fire's natural upward motion. The author argues they are identical in essence despite different movers (coercer vs nature). This illustrates the point concretely.
This example is central to the argument.
4
Understand the broader implication
Reflect on how this argument fits into the larger discussion of contrariety and the nature of motion. The passage hints that the real criterion for specific difference in motion is not the mover but the beginning and end points.
See the commentary for further elaboration.

وذلك لا يوجب اختلافاً في الماهية. قلنا : إنهما وإن لم يختلفا بالماهية لكنهما اختلفا بالمبدئية والمنتهائية وهما متقابلان تقابل التضاد وهذا القدر كاف في اختلاف الحركة بالماهية كذا في المباحث المشرقية ) ولا عبرة ) في الوحدة النوعية للحركة ( بوحدة المحرك ( لأن الأمور المختلفة بالنوع قد تشترك في نوع واحد من الأثر ( ولما مر من أن تعدد المحرك ولو بحسب النوع لا يوجب تعددا في الحركة بحسب الشخص ( وإذ لا يوجب ) اختلاف المحرك بحسب النوع ( اختلاف الشخص في الحركة ( فالنوع أولى ) بأن لا يوجبه ذلك الاختلاف لأن ما يوجب اختلاف النوع يوجب اختلاف الشخص بالضرورة من دون عكس كلي ) فحركة الحجر إلى العلو قسراً و( حركة ) النار إليه طبعاً لا يختلف بالنوع من حيث هما كذلك ) أي من حيث استنادهما إلى محركين مختلفين بالنوع أعني القاسر والطبيعة بل هاتان الحركتان متفقتان في الماهية

قوله: (وهذا القدر كاف أي كون موصوف كل واحد منهما مخالفاً بالآخر كاف لاختلاف الحركة في الماهية الواجب في تضاد الحركات أن يكون مبتدأه ومنتهاه مخالفاً بالنوع للأخرى، ولو بالفرض كما سيجيء في بحث التضاد .

قوله : ( كذا في المباحث (المشرقية لعل الحوالة للإشارة إلى أنه غير مرضي عنده لما سيجيء من أن هذا الاختلاف باعتبار مفهوم المبدأ والمنتهى متحققة في كل حركة مستقيمة يخلف حركة أخرى في المبدأ، والمنتهى مع اتحاد المسافة مع أنه لا تضاد بينهما والاتصاف بالمبدئية والمنتهية، فكيف يكون موجباً للتضاد، وسيجيء تحقيقه .

قوله : (بل هاتان إلخ) أشار إلى أن الحيثية تعليلية وليست بتقييدية حتى يستفاد منها اختلافهما بالتنوع من حيثية أخرى .

قوله : ( لكنهما اختلفا بالمبدائية والمنتهائية إلخ ) فإن قلت : هذا جار في كل حركة من مبدأ إلى منتهى والرجوع إلى ذلك المبدأ مع أنهم قد صرحوا بأن الاختلاف بالماهية ليس إلا في الصاعدة والهابطة قلت لما كان مبدأ الصعود والهبوط ومنتهاهما جهتين حقيقتين لا يتبدلان أصلاً فلا يصير العلو سفلاً. وبالعكس بخلاف سائر الجهات اعتبر ذلك، ولهذا لا يمكن اعتبار الصاعدة هابطة وبالعكس بخلاف الحركة يمنة ويسرة وسيشير الشارح في أثناء المقصد الثامن إلى هذا السؤال وجوابه. واعلم أن قياس العربية كما نقل عن الشارح المنتهية كالمصطفية وأما المصطفوي فقد قيل من الأغلاظ المشهورة كالمرتضوي . قوله : (بل هاتان الحركتان (متفقتان فائدة هذا الكلام دفع توهم نشأ من قوله : لا يختلف بالنوع من حيث هما كذلك لأنه يتبادر منه رجوح النفي إلى قيد الحيثية مع ثبوت أصل الاختلاف النوعي باعتبار آخر فصرح بأن ليس المراد ذلك .
The Fourth Observatory - Sixth Objective: The relation of motion to unity and its types. That does not necessitate a difference in essence. We said: Although they do not differ in essence, they differ in [the] principiality and finality, and they are opposites with the opposition of contrariety, and this amount is sufficient for the difference of motion in essence, as in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya. And no consideration is given, in the specific unity of motion, to the unity of the mover, because things differing in species may share in one type of effect. And because it has passed that the multiplicity of the mover, even according to species, does not necessitate a multiplicity in motion according to the individual. And since difference of the mover according to species does not necessitate difference of the individual in motion, then species is more worthy that that difference does not necessitate it, because what necessitates difference of species necessarily necessitates difference of the individual, without full converse. So the motion of the stone upward by force and the motion of fire upward by nature do not differ in species from the perspective that they are such—i.e., from the perspective of their being based on two movers different in species, namely the coercer and nature—rather these two motions are identical in essence. His saying: 'This amount is sufficient' means that the description of each one of them as being opposite by the other is sufficient for the difference of motion in essence, it being necessary in the contrariety of motions that their beginning and their end be different in species from the other, even if by assumption, as will come in the discussion of contrariety. His saying: 'as in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya'—perhaps the reference indicates that it is not satisfactory to him because of what will come that this difference in terms of the concept of beginning and end is realized in every rectilinear motion that succeeds another motion in beginning and end with unity of distance, while there is no contrariety between them and the description of being a beginning and an end, so how could it be a cause of contrariety? And its verification will come. His saying: 'Rather these two [motions] etc.' indicated that the aspect is explanatory and not restrictive so that their difference in type from another aspect would be inferred. His saying: 'But they differed in principiality and finality etc.' If you say: This applies to every motion from a beginning to an end and the return to that beginning, while they have stated that difference in essence is only in the ascending and descending. I say: Because the beginning of ascent and descent and their ends are two real directions that do not change at all, so the height does not become low and vice versa, unlike other directions; that is considered, and for this reason it is not possible to consider the ascending as descending and vice versa, unlike motion to the right and left, and the commentator will indicate in the course of the eighth objective this question and its answer. And know that the analogy of the Arabic—as transmitted from the commentator—the final is like al-Muṣṭafiyya, and as for al-Muṣṭafawī, it is said to be from the well-known solecisms like al-Murtaḍawī. His saying: 'Rather these two motions are identical'—the benefit of this speech is to repulse a misconception arising from his saying: 'they do not differ in species from the perspective that they are such', because it appears from it the preponderance of negation to the qualification of the aspect with the affirmation of the original specific difference from another consideration, so he declared that that is not intended.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the relation of the unity of the mover to the specific unity of motion. The author begins by stating that difference in the mover does not necessitate a difference in the essence of motion. He then presents a counter-argument: although two motions may have different movers (e.g., one by force, one by nature), they do not differ in essence because the difference lies only in the principiality and finality, which are opposite in a way that constitutes contrariety, but this is sufficient for a difference in essence. However, the author refutes this by saying that the unity of the mover is not a criterion for the specific unity of motion, since things different in species can share the same type of effect. He further notes that multiplicity of the mover, even in species, does not cause multiplicity of motion in the individual, and by extension, species difference in movers does not necessitate species difference in motion. He provides the example of a stone's forced upward motion and fire's natural upward motion: they are identical in essence despite different movers. The commentary then elaborates on the details: it explains that the description of being a beginning or an end is not sufficient to cause contrariety, as it applies to any rectilinear motion. The commentary also addresses a potential objection about ascent and descent being the only cases where essence differs, explaining that their directions are real and immutable. Finally, the commentary clarifies that the author's statement that the motions do not differ in species 'from that perspective' is meant to negate the misconception that they differ in another respect; rather, they are identical in essence.

Correction: The passage shows that two motions from different movers (force vs nature) can be identical in essence, so the mover's identity is not decisive for the species of motion.

Checkpoint

D) The motion of a black object and a white object.

Key Takeaway
Two motions can be identical in essence even if their movers differ in species, as shown by the forced motion of a stone and the natural motion of fire.

Unity of Subject Does Not Specify Motion
Argument that unity of subject does not necessitate specific difference in motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the unity of the subject does not necessitate a specific difference in motion, and that the intrinsic determinants (what it is in, from, and to) are what define motion's essence.

Core Idea
The subject's unity does not dictate motion's type; intrinsic determinants do.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential confusion: if motion occurs in different subjects, does that automatically make the motions different in type? He argues no, using the example of blackness. He then explains that the relation to the subject is external, but the relation to the intrinsic 'container' and 'limits' of motion is internal, thus specifying its essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the main claim and example
Start by reading the passage's opening statement that diversity of substrate does not necessitate diversity of state. Note the example: blackness of a human and a donkey are one type; similarly motion is one type if the intrinsic determinants are the same.
The author uses 'blackness' as a clear case: subjects differ but the accident is the same type.
2
Understand the reason
The author explains that the relation of motion (or any accident) to the subject is external to its essence. Therefore, difference in subjects (substrates) does not cause difference in the accident's essence. This is why blackness remains one type across species.
The key term is 'إضافة' (relation) being 'خارج عن ماهيته' (external to its essence).
3
Consider the objection and its answer
An objection is raised: if the relation to the subject is external, similarly the relation to what the motion is 'in', 'from', and 'to' might also be external. The answer distinguishes: the intrinsic determinants (what it is in, from, to) are constituent of motion's essence because motion is defined as gradual emergence from potency to act. Thus, their difference changes the type of motion, unlike the subject.
This shows that motion's quiddity includes its spatial and directional limits.
4
Connect to broader kalam reasoning
Reflect on the significance: this argument is used in kalam to show that accidents are defined by their own intrinsic properties, not by the subjects they inhere in. This principle helps avoid conflating different types of motion when analyzing physical changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ولا ) عبرة أيضاً ( بوحدة ما له الحركة ( فإن تنوع المحل لا يوجب تنوع الحال وإن كان تعدد المحل مطلقاً يوجب تعدد الحال بحسب الشخص ) فسواد الإنسان و سواد (الحمار نوع واحد ) وكذا حركتهما إذ لم يختلف هناك ما فيه وما منه وما إليه، وذلك لأن إضافة الحركة بل العرض مطلقا إلى الموضوع أمر خارج عن ماهيتهما، فلا يكون اختلاف المعروضات موجباً لاختلافهما

قوله: ( وذلك لأن إضافة الحركة إلخ ) فإن قلت : فكذا إضافتها إلى ما فيه وما منه وما إليه، أمر خارج عن ماهيتها، فكيف يوجب اختلافها بالماهية قلت : لما كانت الحركة خروج الشيء من القوة إلى الفعل تدريجياً كان ما فيه وما إليه وما فيه مقوم ماهيتها، فاختلافها يوجب ماهيتها بخلاف المحرك والمتحرك، فإنها تحتاج إليهما في الوجود في الشفاء، ففي الحركة يختلف نوعيتها باختلاف الأمور التي تقوم ماهيتها وبه ما هي فيه، وأيضاً ما منه وما إليه، فإذا هذه اختلف الحركة في النوع .
And no consideration is given also to the unity of that which has motion, for the diversity of the substrate does not necessitate diversity of the state, although multiplicity of the substrate absolutely necessitates multiplicity of the state according to the individual. So the blackness of a human and the blackness of a donkey are one type, and likewise their motion, since there there is no difference in what is in, from, and to it. That is because the relation of motion—rather the accident absolutely—to the subject is something external to their essence, so difference of the substrates does not necessitate their difference. His saying: 'That is because the relation of motion etc.'—if you say: Similarly, its relation to what is in, from, and to it is something external to its essence, so how does it necessitate its difference in essence? I say: Because motion is the gradual emergence of a thing from potency to act, what is in, to, and in it are constituent of its essence, so their difference necessitates its essence, unlike the mover and the moved, for motion needs them in existence (in al-Shifā'), so in motion its type differs by the difference of the things that constitute its essence and by which it is what it is in, and also what from and to it; so if these differ, motion differs in type.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the unity of the subject (what has motion). The author states that the diversity of the substrate does not necessitate a diversity of the state (i.e., the accident). However, multiplicity of the substrate does necessitate multiplicity of the state according to the individual. He gives the example of blackness: the blackness of a human and the blackness of a donkey are the same type, and similarly their motions are of the same type as long as the intrinsic determinants ('what is in, from, and to it') do not differ. The reason is that the relation of motion (and any accident) to the subject is external to its essence, so difference in substrates does not cause difference in the accident. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: if the relation to the substrate is external, then the relation to the intrinsic determinants (what is in, from, to) should also be external. The answer is that these intrinsic determinants are actually constituent of motion's essence because motion is defined as the gradual emergence from potency to act; thus, the 'what it is in', 'what from', and 'what to' are part of its quiddity. Therefore, their difference causes a difference in the type of motion, unlike the mover and the moved which are only needed for existence in al-Shifā'.

Checkpoint

A) The blackness of a human and a donkey.
B) The motion of a stone by force and fire by nature.
C) The motion of a sphere and a plane.
D) The motion of a heavy and light object.

Key Takeaway
The type of motion is determined by its intrinsic 'from', 'to', and 'in' components, not by the subject it inheres in.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage teaches that the essence of an accident is independent of its subject. In your life, do you tend to define yourself by external circumstances (your job, your status, your relationships) rather than by your inner character? How can you shift your focus to intrinsic qualities that truly constitute who you are?
?
Consider the objection in the passage: we might think the relation to subject is arbitrary, but the relation to intrinsic determinants is essential. Are there areas in your faith practice where you focus on external affiliations (which school, which scholar) rather than the intrinsic truths of the deen? How can you ensure your worship is grounded in substance rather than form?

Time and Generic Unity in Motion
The author discusses unity of time and generic unity in motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the unity of time does not necessitate the specific unity of motion and how generic unity of motion depends on the category (jins).

Core Idea
Time is an accident and measure, not a determinant of motion's specific unity.

In this passage, the author continues his analysis of what can unify motion. After discussing specific unity, he examines the role of time and generic unity. He argues that time, being a single type whose reality does not differ, cannot account for specific unity—only individual unity. He then turns to generic unity, which is based on the category in which motion occurs. This lesson will help you distinguish these different senses of unity and understand why the author dismisses time as irrelevant for specific unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and the translation. Notice the author's argument that time is one type and therefore cannot provide specific unity.
The passage is dense; read slowly and refer to the commentary.
2
Identify the three unities mentioned
The passage refers to specific unity, unity of time, and generic unity. Trace how each is defined and why time is dismissed.
The author says 'وثالثها الوحدة الجنسية'—the third is generic unity.
3
Understand why time is an accident
The author says time is an accident (عارض) and a measure (مقدار). Consider the objection about the motion of the supreme sphere and how it is resolved.
The commentary clarifies that time measures motion, except for the motion of the supreme sphere which is time itself.
4
Grasp generic unity
Generic unity depends on the category (ما فيه) in which motion occurs. For example, motion in place is a genus of motion.
The passage ends with an incomplete thought about non-genericity, but the main point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ولا ) بوحدة الزمان لأنه نوع واحد لا تختلف حقيقته فلا فائدة في اعتبار وحدته النوعية في وحدة الحركة بحسب النوع، بخلاف اعتبار وحدته الشخصية في وحدتها الشخصية ( إن قدر تنوعه) واختلافه بالماهية فهو عارض للحركة ومقدار لها ) واختلاف العوارض) بالنوع ) لا يوجب التنوع في المعروضات، كما أن تنوع المعروضات لا يوجب تنوع عوارضها ) وثالثها الوحدة ( الجنسية وما يعتبر فيها من الوحدات ) بعض ما يعتبر في ( الوحدة ( النوعية ) لأن النوع هو هو الجنس مع قيود منوعة له ( وإنما هو ) وحدة ( ما فيه فقط فالحركة الواقعة في كل جنس جنس من الحركة

قوله: ( فهو عارض للحركة ومقدار لها أي بقدر الحركة بها فيقال : حركة ساعة أو ساعتين، فلا يرد ما قيل : إن الحركة التي اعتبر في اختلافها الزمان غير حركة الزمان مقدار لها، فإنها حركة الفلك الأعظم.

قوله: ( فالحركة الواقعة في كل جنس إلخ) سواء قلنا : إن الحركة المطلقة للحركات على أن تكون الحركات مقولة برأسها أو تكون داخلة في إحدى المقولات أو تكون الحركة في كل مقولة عين تلك المقولات فإن الحركة يختلف بالجنس بسبب اختلاف المقولات الواقعة فيه .

قوله: ( فهو عارض للحركة قيل : هذا ضعيف فإن هذا التعلق بالزمان غير تعلق الحركة التي جعل الزمان عارضاً لها فإنها إنما هي حركة الفلك الأعظم ولو قيل: وإن قدر تنوعه فلا خفاء في جواز إحاطتها بحقيقة واحدة لم يرد هذا ويمكن أن يجاب بأن مجموع الزمان يتقدر به حركة الفلك الأعظم وأما أجزاء الزمان فقد يتقدر بها سائر الحركات أيضاً كما أشار إليه الشارح في بحث الزمان، ولهذا ينقسم الزمان بحسب انقسام الحركة مطلقاً كما سيأتي، وهذا التقدر .. المراد بالعروض هاهنا .

قوله: ( فالحركة الواقعة في كل جنس جنس من الحركة فالحركات الأينية إلخ) لا خفاء أن القول بأن الوحدة الجنسية للحركة يتوقف على وحدة ما فيه جنسها مما يتم إذا ثبت عدم جنسية
And [no consideration is given] to the unity of time, because it is one type whose reality does not differ, so there is no benefit in considering its specific unity in the specific unity of motion, unlike considering its individual unity in its individual unity. If its diversity and difference in essence is supposed, then it is an accident of motion and a measure for it. And the difference of accidents in species does not necessitate diversity in the substrates, just as the diversity of substrates does not necessitate diversity of their accidents. And the third is generic unity, and what is considered in it of the unities is some of what is considered in specific unity, because the species is the genus itself with differentiating restrictions, and it is only the unity of 'what it is in' alone. So motion occurring in every genus is a genus of motion. His saying: 'It is an accident of motion and a measure for it' means according to the measure of motion by it, so one says 'motion of an hour' or 'two hours', and it is not refuted by what is said: that the motion which is considered in its difference of time is not the motion of time its measure, for it is the motion of the supreme sphere. His saying: 'So motion occurring in every genus etc.'—whether we say that absolute motion is a category for motions, such that motions are a category in themselves, or that they are included in one of the categories, or that motion in each category is identical to those categories—motion differs by genus due to the difference of the categories occurring in it. His saying: 'It is an accident of motion'—it is said: this is weak, for this attachment to time is not the attachment of motion which made time an accident for it, for it is only the motion of the supreme sphere. And if it is said: even if its diversity is supposed, there is no concealment of the permissibility of its comprising a single reality, this is not refuted. And it can be answered that the totality of time is measured by the motion of the supreme sphere, and as for parts of time, they may be measured by other motions as well, as the commentator indicated in the discussion of time, and for this reason time is divided according to the division of motion absolutely, as will come. And this measurement is what is intended by accident here. His saying: 'So motion occurring in every genus is a genus of motion, so motions of place etc.'—it is not hidden that the statement that generic unity of motion depends on the unity of its genus of 'what it is in' is completed only if the non-genericity is established.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the roles of time and generic unity in determining the specific unity of motion. The author begins by stating that the unity of time is irrelevant for the specific unity of motion because time is one type whose reality does not vary; only its individual unity matters for the individual unity of motion. If time were to differ in essence, it would be an accident of motion and a measure for it. The author then notes that a difference in accidents does not cause diversity in substrates, and vice versa. He then introduces the third type of unity: generic unity, which is based on the unity of 'what it is in' (mā fīhi), i.e., the category. Thus, motion in each genus is a genus of motion. The commentary elaborates that time is an accident and a measure, giving the example of 'motion of an hour' or 'two hours'. It addresses a possible objection regarding the motion of the supreme sphere, which is not measured by time but rather is time itself. The commentary also explains that the generic unity of motion depends on the unity of what it is in (the category), such that motions differ by genus due to differences in categories (e.g., motion in place, motion in quality). The final part discusses a potential weakness in the claim that time is an accident, but resolves it by distinguishing between the motion of the supreme sphere (which is time) and other motions measured by it.

Correction: The passage clarifies that time is an accident and its generic unity does not affect the essence of motion; only the category (jins) provides generic unity.

C) Time determines the species of motion.
D) Time is the mover of motion.

Key Takeaway
Time, being a single type, is irrelevant for the specific unity of motion; only the category (jins) provides generic unity of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you measuring your spiritual progress by the clock—by how much time you spend in worship—while neglecting the essential change in your heart's orientation towards Allah? This lesson teaches that time is an accident, not the substance of motion. What is the quality of your movement towards Him beyond the hours?
?
The passage argues that differences in accidents do not necessitate diversity in the substrate. Is your identity as a believer—your essential submission—consistent despite the many temporal accidents of joy, sorrow, ease, and hardship? Or do you let changing circumstances shake the core of your faith?

Personal and Specific Unity of Motion
Al-Mawaqif explains personal and specific unity of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between personal and specific unity of motion and how they depend on factors like subject, source, goal, and time.

Core Idea
Personal unity depends on individual subject; specific unity depends on source, goal, and category.

Al-Mawaqif begins by explaining that the diversity of motion comes from its conditions, not just from multiplicity of subjects. He gives a concrete example of blackness and motion to show that two motions can be of the same type even if their subjects differ, as long as the source, goal, and category are the same. This lesson unpacks the first two types of unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing to analyze. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning. Note the technical terms: 'what-it-is-in', 'what-it-is-from', 'what-it-is-to'.
These terms (ما فيه، ما منه، ما إليه) are central to the discussion.
2
Understand the example of blackness
The author compares the blackness of a human and a donkey. Even though the subjects differ, the blackness is the same type because the accidents share the same source, goal, and subject category. The same applies to motion.
This illustrates specific unity, not personal.
3
Examine the role of time
The passage distinguishes between personal and specific unity regarding time. Personal unity considers the individual moment, while specific unity is unaffected by time because time itself is a single type.
This shows that personal unity is more particular.
4
Consider the accident argument
The author argues that difference in quiddity (essence) of motion would be an accident, not a change in the subject. This reinforces that specific unity is determined by the internal aspects of motion, not the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد السادس : تعلق الحرك بالوحدة وأنواعها يوجب تنوع الحال وإن كان تعدد المحل مطلقاً يوجب تعدد الحال بحسب الشخص فسواد الإنسان وسواد الحمار نوع واحد وكذا حركتهما إذ لم يختلف هناك ما فيه وما منه وما إليه وذلك لأن إضافة الحركة بل العرض مطلقا إلى الموضوع أمر خارج عن ماهيتهما فلا يكون اختلاف المعروضات موجباً لاختلافهما ولا بوحدة الزمان لأنه نوع واحد لا تختلف حقيقته فلا فائدة في اعتبار وحدته النوعية في وحدة الحركة بحسب النوع بخلاف اعتبار وحدته الشخصية في وحدتها الشخصية إن قدر تنوعه واختلافه بالماهية فهو عارض للحركة ومقدار لها واختلاف العوارض بالنوع لا يوجب التنوع في المعروضات كما أن تنوع المعروضات لا يوجب تنوع عوارضها وثالثها الوحدة الجنسية وما يعتبر فيها من الوحدات بعض ما يعتبر في الوحدة النوعية لأن النوع هو هو الجنس مع قيود منوعة له وإنما هو وحدة ما فيه فقط فالحركة الواقعة في كل جنس جنس من الحركة فالحركات الأينية كلها متحدة في الجنس العالي وكذا الحركات الكيفية والكمية ويترتب أجناس الحركات بحسب ترتب الأجناس التي تقع تلك الحركات فيها فالحركة في الكيف جنس هو فوق الحركة في الكيفيات المحسوسة وهي جنس فوق الحركة في المبصرات وهي جنس فوق الحركة في الألوان وهكذا إلى أن ننتهي إلى الحركات النوعية المنتهية إلى الحركات الشخصية
The Fourth Observatory - The Sixth Section: The relation of motion to unity and its types necessitates diversity of state. If plurality of subject is absolute, it necessitates diversity of state according to the individual. Thus the blackness of Zayd and the blackness of a donkey are one type, and likewise their motions, since there is no difference in the what-it-is-in, what-it-is-from, and what-it-is-to. That is because the attribution of motion—indeed, of an accident absolutely—to a subject is external to the quiddities of motion and accident. Therefore difference of subjects does not necessitate difference of motion and accident. Nor by the unity of time, because time is one type whose reality does not differ, so considering its specific unity in the specific unity of motion is of no benefit, unlike considering its personal unity in the personal unity of motion. If its diversification and difference in quiddity is supposed, it is an accident of motion and a measure for it. Differences of accidents by type do not necessitate diversification in the subjects, just as diversification of subjects does not necessitate diversification of their accidents. The third is generic unity, and what is considered in it of unities is some of what is considered in specific unity, because the species is the genus itself with differentiating restrictions. It is only the unity of the what-it-is-in only. Thus motion occurring in every genus is a genus of motion. Hence, local motions are all united in the highest genus, as are qualitative and quantitative motions. The genera of motions are arranged according to the arrangement of the genera in which those motions occur. Thus motion in quality is a genus above motion in sensible qualities, and it is a genus above motion in visible things, and it is a genus above motion in colors, and so on until we reach the specific motions ending in individual motions.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage from al-Mawaqif classifies the types of unity of motion. It begins by stating that the diversity of motion depends on the diversity of its conditions, not simply on the plurality of subjects. It gives the example of the blackness of a human and the blackness of a donkey being the same type, and similarly their motions, because the 'what-it-is-in' (the category of place), 'what-it-is-from' (the starting point), and 'what-it-is-to' (the endpoint) are the same. The attribution of motion to a subject is external to the quiddity of motion, so difference of subjects does not necessitate difference of motion. Then it discusses three types of unity: (1) personal unity (unity of the individual subject), (2) specific unity (unity of type), and (3) generic unity (unity of genus). For specific unity, the author notes that time does not affect specific unity because time is a single type; but personal unity considers the individual time. For generic unity, it states that motions are unified at the highest genus level (e.g., all local motions are one genus). The passage then explains how genera of motions are arranged hierarchically: motion in quality is a genus above motion in sensible qualities, and so on down to individual motions.

ما إليه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the unity of motion is entirely determined by the subject (the moving body).
Correction: Al-Mawaqif shows that the subject alone does not determine unity; the source, goal, and category also matter. Two motions with different subjects can be of the same type if these other conditions are the same.

Checkpoint

B) Because their what-it-is-in, what-it-is-from, and what-it-is-to are the same
C) Because they occur at the same time
D) Because their subjects are of the same genus

Key Takeaway
Personal unity is tied to the individual subject and time, while specific unity depends on the category, source, and goal of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you judge the unity of your actions by their source (command of Allah) and goal (seeking His pleasure) rather than by the external identity of the doer? If not, how can you shift your focus from yourself to the purpose of your deeds?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you consider the personal unity of your time—seeing each moment as connected and accountable before Allah? Or do you view your deeds as isolated events? This lesson teaches that personal unity of time benefits personal unity of motion: are your moments fragmented or unified in pursuit of your ultimate goal?

Generic Unity of Motion
Al-Mawaqif describes generic unity and the hierarchy of motion genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand generic unity of motion and the hierarchical arrangement of motion genera based on the subject category.

Core Idea
Generic unity groups motions by the category of the subject (what-it-is-in), forming a hierarchical ladder.

After discussing personal and specific unity, al-Mawaqif introduces the third and broadest type: generic unity. This type considers only the category of the subject—the 'what-it-is-in'—and creates a hierarchy from highest genus down to individual motions. This lesson explains how motions are grouped into genera and species.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on generic unity
Read the Arabic text and translation. Focus on the concept of 'what-it-is-in' as the sole criterion for generic unity.
Generic unity is broader than specific unity; it ignores source and goal.
2
Understand the hierarchy
The author gives examples: local motions are one highest genus, qualitative motions another, etc. Then within quality, there are sub-genera like motion in sensible qualities, motion in visible things, motion in colors, down to specific and personal motions.
Think of an inverted tree: highest genera branch into lower genera, then species, then individuals.
3
Apply the concept
Try to think of other examples: motion in quantity (increase/decrease) is a genus, and within that, growth and diminution are species, etc.
This shows how the classification works in practice.

Contrary and Non-Contrary Motions in Kalam
Al-Mawaqif defines contrary motions and states they only exist between true species under the same ultimate genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion for determining contrary motions and why motions of different genera are not contrary.

Core Idea
Contrariety between motions requires sharing the same ultimate genus.

In this passage from al-Mawaqif, the author clarifies a key point about motion: not all incompatible motions are contrary. He recalls the general principle that contrariety exists only between true species under the same ultimate genus, and then applies it to motion types. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications for classifying motions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument. Then read the English translation. Notice the author's structure: he states the principle, then applies it to motions.
2
Identify the three motions listed as non-contrary
Find the three examples: translation (النقلة), alteration (الاستحالة), and growth (النمو). Understand that they are considered different genera of motion.
These are not the only non-contrary motions, but they are key examples.
3
Understand why they are not contrary
The passage explains they can coexist in one subject at one time. Even if sometimes prevented, the prevention is due to external causes, not their essences. Therefore, they are not truly contrary.
This illustrates the principle that contrariety requires essential incompatibility, not merely occasional prevention.
4
Recognize the condition for true contrariety
Contrariety exists only between motions that are homogeneous and share the same ultimate genus, like heating and cooling within alteration. This is the key takeaway.
Recall previous discussions on opposition to reinforce this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السابع : الحركات المتضادة وغير المتضادة الحركات منها ما هي غير متضادة ومنها ما هي متضادة وقد علمت في مباحث التقابل أن لا تضاد إلا بين الأنواع الحقيقية الداخلة تحت جنس أخير فالحركات المختلفة بالجنس كالنقلة والاستحالة والنمو غير متضادة لأنها أجناس تجتمع في موضوع واحد في زمان واحد وإن امتنع اجتماعهما حيناً فلا لماهياتها أي ليس امتناعها من الاجتماع في ذلك الحين مستندا إلى ماهياتها بل إلى أسباب خارجية فلا تضاد بين الحركات المتخالفة الأجناس وإنما التضاد بين المتجانسة المتشاركة في الجنس الأخير منها ففي الاستحالة
The Seventh Section: Contrary and Non-Contrary Motions. Among motions, some are non-contrary and some are contrary. It has been learned in the investigations of opposition that there is no contrariety except between true species that fall under an ultimate genus. Thus motions differing in genus, such as translation, alteration, and growth, are non-contrary, because they are genera that coexist in one subject at one time, even if their coexistence is prevented at some time—it is not due to their quiddities, i.e., the prevention of their coexistence at that time is not attributed to their quiddities, but to external causes. So there is no contrariety between motions of different genera. Contrariety is only between homogeneous motions that share the ultimate genus, such as within alteration.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage classifies motions into contrary and non-contrary. It begins by recalling a principle from the topic of opposition: contrariety exists only between true species that fall under an ultimate genus. Then it applies this to motions. Motions that differ in genus—like translation (change of place), alteration (change of quality), and growth (change of quantity)—are not contrary, because they are different genera and can coexist in the same subject at the same time (e.g., a body can grow and change color simultaneously). If they cannot coexist at some time, that is due to external causes, not to their essentieces. Therefore, no contrariety exists between motions of different genera. Contrariety is only found between motions that are homogeneous and share the same ultimate genus, such as the contrariety within the species of alteration (e.g., heating and cooling). The passage thus provides a precise criterion for determining contrary motions.

النقلة
الاستحالة
النمو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two motions that cannot coexist at the same time are contrary.
Correction: Contrariety only exists between motions of the same ultimate genus; incompatibility due to external factors does not make them contrary if they belong to different genera.

Key Takeaway
True contrariety between motions requires them to be species of the same ultimate genus; motions of different genera are not contrary even if they occasionally cannot coexist.

Opposing and Non-Opposing Movements in the Three Categories
Defines opposing and non-opposing movements with examples from three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition for opposition between movements and why certain pairs like blackening/whitening, growth/decay, and ascending/descending are opposing, while movements of different genera and positional movement are not.

Core Idea
Opposition between movements only exists between species under the same final genus.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key distinction in the study of motion: not all movements that cannot coexist are truly opposing. He defines the condition for genuine opposition and illustrates it with concrete examples from the categories of quality, quantity, and locomotion. This prepares the ground for understanding how movement relates to change and substance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction
Read the opening lines to grasp the basic division: movements are either opposing or non-opposing. The key is the condition of sharing the same final genus.
Opposition is not simply incompatibility.
2
Learn the condition
The text states that opposition only exists between true species under a final genus. Movements of different genera are non-opposing because they can coexist by their essences.
External causes may prevent coexistence, but that doesn't make them opposing.
3
Study examples in istihala (qualitative change)
Focus on the example of blackening and whitening. Note that they share the final genus of color movement and have extreme difference.
This is the paradigm case of opposing movements.
4
Study examples in quantity and locomotion
Examine the pairs: growth/decay, rarefaction/condensation, ascending/descending. Each has defined opposite limits under the same genus.
The same logic applies to these categories.
5
Note the exception for positional movement
The author concludes that positional movement (e.g., circular) has no opposition, as will be explained later.
This shows that not all categories of movement admit opposition.

المقصد السابع : الحركات المتضادة وغير المتضادة ] ( ) الحركات منها ما هي غير متضادة ومنها ( ما هي متضادة وقد علمت ) في مباحث التقابل ( أن لا تضاد إلا بين الأنواع الحقيقية (الداخلة تحت جنس أخير فالحركات المختلفة بالجنس كالنقلة والاستحالة والنمو غير متضادة ) لأنها أجناس تجتمع في موضوع واحد في زمان واحد وإن امتنع اجتماعها حيناً) من الأحيان (فلا لماهياتها) أي ليس امتناعها من الاجتماع في ذلك الحين مستندا إلى ماهياتها، بل إلى أسباب خارجية فلا تضاد بين الحركات المتخالفة الأجناس ( وإنما التضاد بين المتجانسة ( المتشاركة في الجنس الأخير ( منها ) أي من الحركات (ففي الاستحالة
قوله : (وإن امتنع اجتماعهما حيناً فلا لماهياتها كالوقوف في النمو فإن الجسم حينئذ لو تحرك في الكيف أو الوضع ولم يتحرك في النمو لا يكون لأجل التضاد .
في الاستحالة كالتسود والتبيض ) فإنهما نوعان مندرجان تحت الحركة في الألوان ومتشاركان في الموضوع وبينهما من الخلاف ما هو هو أكثر مما وبين التصفر والتحمر وغيرهما فهو غاية الخلاف ولا معنى للتضاد إلا ذلك ) وفي الكم كالنمو والذبول والتخلخل والتكاثف فإن لكل واحد من النمو والذبول حداً محدوداً في الطبع يتوجهان إليه وبينهما غاية الخلاف فكذا بين الحركتين إليهما وكذا الحال في التخلخل والتكاثف إذ لكل واحد منهما حد لا يتجاوزه ( وفي النقلة كالصاعدة والهابطة) فإن كل واحد من الصعود والهبوط له حد محدود وبينهما غاية الخلاف وإلى ما فصلناه أشار إجمالا بقوله : (إذ لها أي للحركة في هذه المقولات الثلاثة في كل طرف حد محدود تتوجه إليه وبين الطرفين غاية الخلاف ) فإن السواد والبياض بينهما غاية الخلاف وكذا بين حدي النمو والذبول والتخلخل والتكاثف والصعود والهبوط فيكون بين التوجهين أيضاً غاية الخلاف ( وأما ) الحركة ( الوضعية فلا تضاد فيها )
لما ستعرف عن قريب من أن الحركة المستديرة لا تضاد فيها .
The seventh observation: Opposing and non-opposing movements. ( ) Movements: some are non-opposing and some are opposing. And you have learned in the investigations of opposition that opposition only exists between true species (falling under a final genus). So movements differing in genus, like locomotion (al-naqla), qualitative change (al-istiḥāla), and growth (al-namū), are non-opposing, because they are genera that coexist in one subject at one time, even if their coexistence is sometimes prevented. That prevention is not due to their essences, but to external causes. So there is no opposition between movements of different genera. Rather, opposition is between homogeneous movements sharing the final genus. Among them, i.e., movements, in qualitative change: like blackening and whitening; for they are two species under movement in colors, sharing the subject, and between them the difference is greater than that between yellowing and reddening and others, so it is extreme difference, and there is no meaning for opposition except that. And in quantity: like growth and decay, rarefaction and condensation. For each of growth and decay has a defined limit in nature toward which they move, and between them is extreme difference, and likewise between the two movements toward them. And similarly for rarefaction and condensation, since each has a limit it does not exceed. And in locomotion: like ascending and descending. For each of ascent and descent has a defined limit, and between them is extreme difference. And to what we have detailed, he alluded summarily by his saying: 'For movement in these three categories, in each extreme there is a defined limit toward which it moves, and between the two extremes is extreme difference.' For blackness and whiteness have extreme difference between them, and likewise between the limits of growth and decay, rarefaction and condensation, and ascent and descent, so between the two tendencies there is also extreme difference. (And as for) positional movement, there is no opposition in it, because you will soon know that circular movement has no opposition.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage defines opposing and non-opposing movements and gives detailed examples. It begins by stating that opposition only occurs between true species under a final genus. Movements of different genera (e.g., locomotion, qualitative change, growth) are not opposing because they can coexist in the same subject at the same time, even if sometimes prevented due to external causes. True opposition is between homogeneous movements sharing the final genus. The passage then provides examples from three categories: qualitative change (istiḥāla): blackening vs. whitening; quantity: growth vs. decay, rarefaction vs. condensation; locomotion: ascending vs. descending. In each case, the movements have defined opposite extremes. The summary statement clarifies that in each of these three categories, each extreme has a defined limit and the extremes are utterly different, so the movements toward them are opposing. Finally, positional movement (al-ḥaraka al-waḍ‘iyya) is said to have no opposition, because circular movement (as will be explained) has no opposite. The commentary on the first part explains that the prevention of coexistence of movements of different genera is not due to their essences but to external causes, using the example of a body that does not grow while moving in quality or position—this is not due to opposition but to the body's limited capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two movements that cannot occur together are opposites.
Correction: Opposition requires that the movements share the same final genus and have extreme difference; mere incompatibility due to external factors does not constitute opposition.

Checkpoint
Why are movements of different genera like locomotion and qualitative change not considered opposing?
A) Because they occur in different subjects.
B) Because they can coexist in the same subject at the same time by their essences.
C) Because they belong to different categories and cannot be compared.
D) Because opposition only occurs between species under the same final genus.

Key Takeaway
Opposition between movements requires sharing the same final genus and having extreme difference in their limits; this is true for qualitative change, quantity, and locomotion but not for positional movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own thinking, do you frequently assume that any two conflicting ideas are opposites, without checking if they fall under the same 'final genus'? How might this lesson help you avoid false dichotomies and see compatibility where you once saw only conflict?
?
Consider an area of your life where you experience inner struggle — between desires, or between a desire and a duty. Are these truly 'opposing forces' in the kalam sense, or are they different genera that could coexist if you aligned their purpose? What would change if you treated them as non-opposing?
?
The passage says positional movement has no opposite. Is your orientation in life — the direction your heart is set on — a kind of 'positional movement' that has no true opposite? If so, what does that mean for how you pursue or change your life direction?

Opposing Motions in Quality, Quantity, and Transposition
Classifies motions with tadadd in three categories and denies it in positional motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians classify opposing motions into three categories (quality, quantity, transposition) and why positional motion is excluded.

Core Idea
Opposite motions are defined by opposite limits, not by subjects.

The concept of opposition (tadadd) is central to understanding motion in Islamic theology. This passage from al-Ījī’s Mawāqif systematically identifies which types of motion can be truly opposite and why. By focusing on the endpoints or limits of motion, the author clarifies that opposition arises from extreme difference between those limits, not from the moving object itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a classification and ends with a denial.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage presents three categories of motion that have tadadd: quality (الكيف), quantity (الكم), and transposition (النقلة). For each, find the examples given: blackening/whitening, growth/withering, rarefaction/condensation, ascending/descending.
3
Understand the criterion for opposition
For each category, the passage states that each motion has a defined limit (حد محدود) and between the two limits there is extreme difference (غاية الخلاف). Thus, the motions toward those limits are opposites. Notice that this criterion is about the endpoints, not the subject.
The author emphasizes that the meaning of tadadd is precisely this extreme difference.
4
Note the exception for positional motion
The passage explicitly says that positional motion (الحركة الوضعية) has no tadadd, with a brief mention that circular motion, as a kind of positional motion, is not opposed. This is a key exception to the previous three categories.
The author promises a longer explanation later.

The Fourth Observatory - The Eighth Topic: Opposing rising and falling motions. (And in quality, like blackening and whitening) for they are two types falling under motion in colors, sharing the subject, and between them there is more disagreement than between yellowing and reddening and others, so it is the extreme of disagreement, and there is no meaning for opposition except that. And in quantity, like growth and withering, rarefaction and condensation, for each of growth and withering has a defined limit in nature toward which they tend, and between them is extreme disagreement, and likewise between the two motions toward them, and similarly in rarefaction and condensation since each has a limit it does not exceed. And in transposition, like ascending and descending, for each of ascent and descent has a defined limit and between them is extreme disagreement. And to what we have detailed he alluded summarily with his saying: (For it, i.e., for motion in these three categories, in each endpoint there is a defined limit toward which it tends, and between the two endpoints there is extreme disagreement). Indeed, between black and white there is extreme disagreement, and likewise between the limits of growth and withering, rarefaction and condensation, ascent and descent, so between the two tendencies also there is extreme disagreement. (And as for) positional motion, there is no opposition in it, as you will soon know about circular motion having no opposition in it.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage classifies motions that have tadadd (opposition) into three categories: quality (al-kayf), quantity (al-kamm), and transposition (al-naqla). For each, it explains that there are two opposite limits between which there is extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf). In quality, examples are blackening and whitening; in quantity, growth and withering, rarefaction and condensation; in transposition, ascending and descending. Each motion has a definite natural limit it aims for, and between the two limits there is extreme difference, thus the motions toward them are opposites. The passage then notes that positional motion (al-ḥaraka al-waḍʿiyya) has no tadadd, as will be explained later regarding circular motion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that tadadd of motions is due to opposition of the subject.
Correction: The passage shows that tadadd is due to opposition of the limits (endpoints), not the subject. For example, blackening and whitening share the same subject but are opposite because black and white are maximally different.

Key Takeaway
Opposition in motion is defined by extreme difference between limits, not by the moving object.

What concrete change would break that cycle?

Conditions for Contradictory Movements
Al-Jurjani defines contradictory movements and classifies the conditions of their endpoints.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise conditions under which two movements are considered contradictory, based on the opposition of both their starting and ending points.

Core Idea
Two movements are contradictory only if both their start and end points are opposites.

Al-Jurjani carefully defines what makes two movements contradictory, using ascending and descending movements as examples. He explains that it is not enough for only the start or only the end points to be opposites; both must be. He then categorizes three possible relations between endpoints, showing which yield true contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the English translation. Get a sense of the author's argument.
2
Identify the main condition
Look for the statement that defines when two movements are contradictory. Note that both starting and ending points must be opposites.
3
Examine the three cases
The passage lists three ways endpoints can differ: (1) by essence with opposition, (2) by essence without opposition, (3) by accident. Understand which cases lead to contradiction.
Cases 1 and 3 produce contradictory movements; case 2 does not.
4
Understand the 'by accident' case
Focus on the example of center and circumference. They are opposite only due to accidental attributes (nearness and farness). This shows that opposition can be derived from accidental properties.
5
Reflect on the common misconception
Some think opposite direction alone suffices. The passage clarifies that the endpoints themselves must be opposites, not just the direction.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثامن : الحركات المتضادة الصاعدة والهابطة من الأطراف وإليها أعني (بحسب ما منه وما إليه) جميعاً (من حيث هما كذلك) أي من حيث أنهما متضادان أعني أن يكون مبدأ إحدى الحركتين ضداً لمبدأ الأخرى ومنتهاها ضداً لمنتهاها وليس يكفي لتضاد الحركة التضاد المبدأين فقط، فإن الحركة من السواد إلى الحمرة لا تضاد الحركة من البياض إلى الحمرة ولا التضاد بين المنتهيين فقط، فإن الحركة من الحمرة إلى البياض لا تضاد الحركة من الحمرة إلى السواد وذلك لانتفاء غاية الخلاف، وإنما اعتبر قيد الحيثية إذ لا بد من اعتباره (فإنهما) أي ما منه وما إليه في الحركتين (قد يختلفان بالذات) والماهية (مع التضاد بينهما) كالسواد والبياض فالحركة من الأول إلى الثاني تضاد الحركة من الثاني إلى الأول لأن مبدآهما متضادان بالذات وكذلك منتهاهما (أو دونه) أي دون التضاد كالسواد والحمرة فإنهما متخالفان بالماهية بلا تضاد لعدم التباعد في الغاية فلا تضاد أيضاً بين الحركة من أحدهما إلى الآخر وعكسها (أو بالعرض) أي يختلفان لا بالذات بل باعتبار عارض مع التضاد بحسبه أيضاً كالمركز والمحيط لأنهما جزآن (أي نقطتان) من جسم بسيط عرض لأحدهما أنه غاية القرب من [؟] وللآخر أنه غاية البعد عنه (وباعتبار هذين العارضين صارا متضادين مع تساويهما في الحقيقة) تضادها لأجل الحصول في الأطراف بأنه لا تعلق للحركات بذلك الحصول فكيف يعلل تضادها . قوله : ( أعني بحسب ما منه إلخ) ليس التضاد لأجل التوجه من الأطراف، وإليها من حيث أنها أطراف ونهايات لبعد إذ ليس ذلك مما لا يتعلق به الحركة بل لأجل أن تلك الأطراف ماهية الحركة وما إليه ولا كونها ما منه كيف اتفق بل من حيث أنهما متضادان مثلا بعد المركز والمحيط ليست من حيث كونهما طرفين لبعد أو من حيث كون أحدهما غاية البعد عن الآخر موجبتين لتضاد الصاعدة والهابطة، إذ لا تعلق للحركة بها بهذا الاعتبار بل من حيث كونها منه فقوله : كذلك ظرف مستقر وقع حالاً أي التوجه من الأطراف، وإليها من حيث التوجه في الضدين فقول الشارح : من حيث أنهما متضادان بيان الحاصل المعنى . عبارته . قوله من جسم بسيط) أي يحددهما جسم بسيط لا أنهما جزآن منه كما يتبادر من قوله : ( وباعتبار هذين العارضين إلخ
The fourth observation - the eighth objective: The contradictory movements, ascending and descending, from the extremities and to them, meaning (according to what from it and what to it) all together (from the perspective that they are such) i.e., from the perspective that they are two opposites, meaning that the beginning of one movement is opposite to the beginning of the other, and its end is opposite to its end. And it is not sufficient for the contradiction of movement the contradiction of the two beginnings only, for the movement from black to red is not contradictory to the movement from white to red, nor the contradiction between the two ends only, for the movement from red to white is not contradictory to the movement from red to black, due to the absence of extreme opposition. And the condition of the perspective was considered, for it must be considered. (For they) i.e., the starting and ending points in the two movements (may differ by essence) and quiddity (with opposition between them) like black and white, so the movement from the first to the second is contradictory to the movement from the second to the first because their beginnings are essentially opposite, and likewise their ends. (Or less than that) i.e., less than opposition, like black and red, for they are different in quiddity without opposition due to the lack of distance in extremity, so there is no contradiction between the movement from one to the other and its reverse. (Or by accident) i.e., they differ not by essence but due to an accidental attribute with opposition accordingly, such as the center and circumference because they are two parts (i.e., two points) of a simple body, to one of which it accidentally belongs that it is the extreme proximity from [?] and to the other that it is the extreme distance from it (and on account of these two accidental attributes they become opposites) while being equal in reality. Its contradiction is due to being in the extremities, that movements have no relation to that being, so how is their contradiction explained? He said: (I mean according to what from it, etc.) The contradiction is not because of the direction from the extremities and to them in that they are extremities and ends of distance, for that is not something to which movement is related, but because those extremities are the quiddity of the movement and what to it, and not being what from it howsoever, but from the perspective that they are two opposites, for example after the center and circumference, they are not from the perspective of being two sides of a distance or from the perspective that one of them is the extreme distance from the other causing the contradiction of the ascending and descending, for movement has no relation to them in this respect, but from the perspective that it is from it. So his statement: 'such' is a circumstantial adverb, i.e., the direction from the extremities and to them from the perspective of direction in the two opposites. So the commentator's statement: from the perspective that they are two opposites is an explanation of the obtained meaning. His expression. His statement: 'from a simple body' i.e., a simple body delimits them, not that they are two parts of it as apparent from his statement: (And on account of these two accidental attributes, etc.)
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for two movements to be contradictory, specifically ascending and descending. It begins by stating the general condition: the movement must be from one extremity to another, and the opposition must be considered from the perspective that both the starting and ending points are opposites. It then clarifies that it is not enough for only the starting points to be opposites, nor only the ending points; both must be opposites. Examples are given: movement from black to red is not contradictory to movement from white to red because the starting points are not opposites (black and white are opposites, but the ending points are the same). Similarly, movement from red to white is not contradictory to movement from red to black because the ending points are not opposites. The author then classifies the ways in which the starting and ending points may differ: (1) by essence with opposition (e.g., black and white), (2) by essence without opposition (e.g., black and red), and (3) by accident (e.g., center and circumference, which are opposite only due to accidental attributes of being near and far). Only cases (1) and (3) produce contradictory movements. Following this, the commentator explains that the contradiction is not due to the movement being towards extremities per se, but due to the endpoints being opposites. He elaborates on the phrase 'from the perspective that they are such', emphasizing that the opposition must be inherent in the endpoints themselves, not merely in their being endpoints. The passage concludes with a note about the interpretation of 'from a simple body' to clarify that the center and circumference are not parts of the body but are delimited by it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two movements are contradictory if they are in opposite directions (e.g., up and down).
Correction: Opposite direction alone is not enough; the starting and ending points must themselves be opposites. For example, movement from black to red and from white to red are not contradictory because the endpoints are not both opposite.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of two movements that are NOT contradictory because the endpoints are not opposites?
A) Movement from black to white and from white to black
B) Movement from black to red and from white to red
C) Movement from center to circumference and from circumference to center
D) Movement from red to white and from red to black

Key Takeaway
Contradictory movements require opposition of both the starting and ending points, not just the direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, when have you assumed two ideas were contradictory based only on their apparent direction, without checking whether their starting and ending points truly oppose each other? How might this lesson change the way you examine conflicts in your beliefs?
?
The passage distinguishes between essential and accidental opposition. Think of a disagreement you have with someone. Is the opposition essential or accidental? Are you both looking at the same endpoint? What would it mean to hold that standard of precision in that relationship?
?
This lesson teaches that contradiction requires a specific structure. If your heart claims two truths contradict, are you willing to suspend judgment until you examine their endpoints? What is the cost of rushing to declare something contradictory?

When Accidental Opposition Causes Essential Opposition
Al-Jurjani presents an objection and its answer about accidental opposition of endpoints leading to essential opposition of movements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition under which accidental opposition necessitates essential opposition, specifically regarding the movements of ascent and descent.

Core Idea
Accidental opposition can necessitate essential opposition when the definition of opposites applies to the subjects.

This passage addresses a subtle point in the discussion of opposite movements. The objection claims that accidental opposition (like endpoints being opposite) cannot make the movements themselves essentially opposite. Al-Jurjani clarifies that this is not absolute: if the accidental opposition is such that the definition of 'opposites' applies to the subjects, then it does necessitate essential opposition. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify the claim that accidental opposition does not necessitate essential opposition, and the question about how it could apply to movements.
2
Read the answer
Notice the qualification: it is not absolute; if the definition of opposites applies to the subjects, then it does.
The answer uses 'specific such that...'
3
Understand the definition of opposites
Reflect on what makes two things opposites: they cannot coexist in one subject and have extreme difference. How does this apply to the ascending and descending movements?
4
Apply the principle
See that the opposition of endpoints (center and circumference) makes ascending and descending movements truly opposite because they cannot coexist and are extremely different.
The commentator is referenced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد ذكروا أن تضاد العارض لا يوجب تضاد المعروض، فكيف أوجب تضاد عارض بعض ما تتعلق به الحركة تضاد الحركة مع أن هذا أبعد قلنا : مرادهم أن ذلك بمجرده وعلى إطلاقه لا يوجب تضاد المعروض وأما إذا كان بخصوصه بحيث يوجب صدق حد الضدين على المعروض أو ما يتعلق به فلا استبعاد، وهاهنا قد يصدق بتضاد الطرفين حد الضدين على الحركتين، أعني الصاعدة والهابطة كما ذكره الشارح.
For it is said: They have mentioned that the opposition of the accident does not necessitate the opposition of the subject, so how does the opposition of an accident of something related to movement necessitate the opposition of the movement, while this is more remote? We say: Their intended meaning is that this, by itself and in an absolute sense, does not necessitate the opposition of the subject, but when it is specific such that it necessitates the truth of the definition of the two opposites on the subject or on what is related to it, then there is no improbability. And here, by the opposition of the two endpoints, the definition of the two opposites is true of the two movements, namely the ascending and descending, as the commentator mentioned.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the previous argument that accidental opposition of endpoints (like center and circumference) causes essential opposition of the movements (ascending and descending). The objection states that an accident's opposition does not necessitate the opposition of the subject (the thing that bears the accident). For example, if two things are opposite in an accidental quality, the things themselves are not necessarily opposite. So how can the accidental opposition of the endpoints cause the movements themselves to be essentially opposite? The answer explains that this is not a general rule; if the accidental opposition is such that it makes the definition of 'opposites' apply to the subjects, then it does necessitate opposition. In this case, the opposition of the endpoints (center and circumference) as accidental attributes is specific enough that the definition of opposites (two things that cannot coexist in a single subject and have extreme difference) applies to the ascending and descending movements themselves, because they cannot coexist in the same body at the same time and have extreme difference (one goes up, the other down). Thus, the accidental opposition of the endpoints leads to essential opposition of the movements.

Key Terms
تضاد العارض
تضاد المعروض
حد الضدين
تضاد بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that accidental opposition never causes essential opposition.
Correction: The answer clarifies that it can, when the accidental opposition is specific enough that the definition of opposites applies to the subjects.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, when does accidental opposition necessitate essential opposition?
A) Always, because any accident makes the subject opposite
B) Never, because accidents are separate from essence
C) Only when it makes the definition of opposites true of the subjects
D) Only when the accident is necessary and inseparable

Key Takeaway
Accidental opposition can necessitate essential opposition if it makes the definition of opposites true of the subjects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a disagreement within the Muslim community, do you immediately assume the opposition is essential? How does this lesson challenge you to first check whether the definition of opposites truly applies before concluding that differences are irreconcilable? What practical step can you take this week to avoid treating accidental differences as essential divisions?
?
This lesson shows that accidental things (like endpoints) can affect essential nature (like movement) when the definition of opposites is satisfied. Reflect on your own life: what small, seemingly accidental habits or attitudes have gradually shaped your character? Are there any that you need to change because they have become essential to your identity? Commit to one concrete action to address this.

Accidental Opposition as Cause for Essential Opposition
Al-Jurjani explains how accidental opposition of endpoints causes essential opposition in movements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the accidental opposition between two endpoints becomes the cause for the essential opposition between movements like ascending and descending.

Core Idea
Accidental opposition in endpoints can cause essential opposition in movements.

In this lesson, we examine a key principle in al-Jurjani's analysis of opposition: how the accidental relationship between two points (center and circumference) translates into an essential opposition between the movements that connect them. This is crucial for understanding the nature of straight motions, particularly why ascending and descending are fundamentally contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
The passage uses technical terms like 'tadadd bil-'arad' and 'tadadd bil-dhat'.
2
Identify the cause-and-effect link
Notice that al-Jurjani explicitly states that accidental opposition of endpoints causes essential opposition of ascending and descending movements. This is the main point.
The cause is the accidental opposition of the center and circumference.
3
Understand the nature of ascending and descending
These movements are described as 'existential meanings' that cannot coexist in one subject and have extreme difference. Reflect on why this makes them essentially opposite.
This is the effect of the accidental opposition.
4
Reflect on the statement about straight movement
Al-Jurjani mentions that some say there is no contradiction in straight movement except between ascending and descending. He advises to reflect. Consider why other opposite directions might not be contradictory.
This hints at the limiting condition of accidental opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحقيقة) وصار تضادهما بالعرض سبباً لتضاد الصاعدة والهابطة بالذات، فإنهما معنيان وجوديان يمتنع اجتماعهما في موضوع واحد وبينهما غاية الخلاف، وكذا حال الحركتين الواقعتين في جهتين متقابلتين وقد يقال : لا تضاد في الحركة المستقيمة الصاعدة والهابطة فعليك بالتأمل ) وقد لا يختلفان أصلا أي لا يختلف مبدأ الحركة ومنتهاها لا بحسب الماهية ولا بحسب عارض لازم ) بل يتفق إن صار أحدهما مبدأ ) لحركة ) والآخر منتهى ( لها ، فإذا فرض حركة أخرى من هذا المنتهى إلى ذلك المبدأ لم تكن مضادة للأولى، إذ لا تضاد بين المبدأين ولا بين المنتهيين لا بالذات ولا بالعرض فإن قلت بين مفهومي المبدأ والمنتهى تقابل التضاد كما سننبه عليه فبين قوله : (ولا) بحسب عارض (لازم يشعر بأن التضاد في القسم الثاني إنما هو بسبب اختلاف المبدأ والمنتهى بحسب عارض لازم فعلى هذا لاتضاد في الحركة المستقيمة إلا بين الصاعدة والهابطة، وقوله : قلت: لا شك إلخ يشعر بأن عدم تأخر العارض الذي يكون سبباً لتضاد المبدأ والمنتهى يكفي في تضاد الحركة ولو كان مفارقاً، اللهم إلا أن يبنى كلامه على أن هذا العارض ما وجد إلا لازما بالاستقراء .
(The reality) And their opposition by accident became a cause for the opposition of the ascending and descending by essence, for they are two existential meanings that cannot coexist in a single subject and between them is extreme opposition. And likewise the case of the two movements occurring in two opposite directions. And it may be said: There is no contradiction in straight movement except between ascending and descending, so you must reflect. And they may not differ at all, i.e., the beginning of movement and its end do not differ either in quiddity or by a necessary accident, but they coincide such that one becomes a beginning for a movement and the other an end for it. So if another movement is assumed from this end to that beginning, it would not be contradictory to the first, as there is no opposition between the two beginnings nor between the two ends, neither by essence nor by accident. For if you say: Between the concepts of beginning and end there is the opposition of contradiction, as we will point out, then it is clear. His statement: (Nor) by a necessary accident indicates that the opposition in the second case is only due to the difference of the beginning and end by a necessary accident. Accordingly, there is no contradiction in straight movement except between ascending and descending. And his statement: I said: No doubt, etc., indicates that the non-delay of the accident that is the cause for the opposition of the beginning and end suffices for the opposition of the movement, even if it is separable, unless his speech is based on that this accident is not found except as necessary by induction.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage begins by reiterating that the accidental opposition of the endpoints (center and circumference) becomes the cause for the essential opposition of ascending and descending movements. These movements are existential meanings that cannot coexist in a single subject and have extreme difference (one goes up, the other down). The same applies to movements in opposite directions (e.g., left and right). It then notes that some say there is no contradiction in straight movement except between ascending and descending, implying other straight movements are not contradictory. Next, it introduces the case where the starting and ending points do not differ at all—neither in quiddity nor by a necessary accident. In that case, if one point serves as the start of one movement and the end of another, and the second movement goes from that end back to the start, the two movements are not contradictory because there is no opposition between the starts or ends. An objection is raised: but the concepts of 'beginning' and 'end' themselves have the opposition of contradiction (one is opposite to the other conceptually). The response (implicitly) distinguishes between conceptual opposition and the actual opposition of the points themselves. The commentator clarifies that in the second case (the case of no necessary accident), the contradiction is only due to a necessary accident, and if there is no necessary accident, there is no contradiction. The commentator also discusses that the accident causing opposition need not be inseparable; even if separable, it can still cause opposition, though the author's statement suggests that by induction such accidents are only found as necessary.

Key Terms
تضاد بالعرض
تضاد بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the cause of the essential opposition between ascending and descending movements?
A) The difference in their directions
B) The accidental opposition of the center and circumference
C) The fact that they are existential meanings
D) They cannot coexist in one subject

Key Takeaway
Accidental opposition of endpoints can cause essential opposition of movements, as in the case of ascending and descending.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are there relationships or beliefs you treat as fundamentally opposed (essential) when the opposition might actually stem from accidental differences? What would change if you traced the root cause?
?
The author ends with 'fa'alayka bi-al-ta'ammul' (so you must reflect). What is one area of your faith or practice where you have accepted a contradiction without examining its cause? How can you investigate it today?
?
Consider the 'movements' of your heart—your desires and aversions. Could some of them be essentially opposed only because you have attached them to accidentally opposed endpoints? How might reattaching them resolve an inner conflict?

When Beginning and End Do Not Differ
Al-Jurjani describes the case where beginning and end of a movement are identical in quiddity and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two movements are not contradictory if their starting and ending points do not differ in quiddity or by a necessary accident.

Core Idea
No difference in endpoints means no opposition in movements.

In this lesson, we consider a special case: what if the starting point and ending point of a movement are essentially identical? Al-Jurjani explains that in such a scenario, two movements that go back and forth between the same points are not contradictory, because there is no opposition between the endpoints themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the condition described.
2
Identify the condition
The passage states that the beginning and end may not differ at all, neither in quiddity nor by a necessary accident. This is the key condition.
If they differ by a necessary accident, then the case falls under the previous scenario of accidental opposition.
3
Understand the consequence
Under this condition, if you assume a movement from one point to the other and another movement back, they are not contradictory because there is no opposition between the starts or ends.
This contrasts with the ascending/descending case where the endpoints (center and circumference) are opposite.
